### [ExplainSpeaking: Comparison between the economies of Palestine and Israel in 6 charts](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/explained-economics/explainspeaking-the-economies-of-palestine-israel-in-6-charts-9006859/)

This article provides a snapshot comparison of the economies of Israel and Palestine based on data from the International Monetary Fund (IMF) and the World Bank. It highlights several key economic parameters:

1. Population size: Israel's population is almost double that of the West Bank and Gaza.

2. Nominal GDP: Israel's GDP is over 26 times larger than Palestine's.

3. GDP per capita: Israel's average income level is almost 15 times higher than Palestine's.

4. Inflation rate: The inflation rate in Israel is slightly higher than in Palestine.

5. Real GDP growth rate: Palestine's economy fluctuates more than Israel's, with higher vulnerability and weaker growth rates in certain years.

6. Unemployment rate: Palestine has a significantly higher unemployment rate compared to Israel.

The article also discusses the reasons behind Palestine's economic struggles, including systemic restrictions imposed by the Israeli government and a decline in certain sectors such as agriculture, forestry, and fishing. The World Bank predicts continued economic challenges for Palestine due to these constraints and a lack of conclusive reform programs.

Overall, the article highlights the significant disparities between the economies of Israel and Palestine and the ongoing challenges faced by Palestine in achieving economic growth and development.

A new study published in the journal Nature has found that the rapid melting of West Antarctica's ice sheet is now inevitable, regardless of efforts to reduce carbon emissions. The melting of the ice sheet would raise global sea levels by 5.3 meters or 17.4 feet, posing a significant threat to coastal cities worldwide, including those in India. Even under the best-case scenario of limiting global warming to 1.5 degrees Celsius above pre-industrial levels, the water around West Antarctica will continue to warm three times faster than in the 20th century, leading to increased melting of the ice sheet. ### [Rajnath Singh talks about ‘prisoner’s dilemma’ in international relations: What is this concept](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/explained-economics/rajnath-singh-prisoners-dilemma-9006097/)

During his keynote address at the Goa Maritime Conclave, Defence Minister Rajnath Singh highlighted the importance of collaboration and trust between countries in international relations. He referred to the concept of "Prisoner's Dilemma" to explain the strategic decision-making challenges faced by countries. The Prisoner's Dilemma is a game in Game Theory that demonstrates how individuals or entities behave under different circumstances and aims to achieve the best outcome. Singh emphasized the need to find solutions that promote cooperation, build trust, and mitigate the risks associated with the Prisoner's Dilemma situations. The article provides examples of how this concept applies to scenarios such as an arms race between countries or competition between companies in the market. It concludes by suggesting that countries should establish ground rules in geopolitics to avoid detrimental consequences for their economies and people.

### [Rajnath Singh talks about ‘prisoner’s dilemma’ in international relations: What is this concept](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/everyday-explainers/rajnath-singh-prisoners-dilemma-9006097/)

Defence Minister Rajnath Singh spoke about the concept of "Prisoner's Dilemma" during his keynote address at the Goa Maritime Conclave. He explained that this concept can be applied to international relations to understand situations where countries face strategic decision-making challenges. Singh emphasized the need for countries to collaborate and build trust instead of engaging in an arms race out of fear and mistrust. Prisoner's Dilemma is a popular game in Game Theory that helps understand how people or entities behave under different circumstances. It highlights the paradox of whether to confess or stay silent in a situation where two prisoners are questioned about a crime. The best outcome lies in cooperation, but the dilemma has real-life applications in international law and business, such as setting ground rules to avoid an arms race or agreeing on prices to ensure healthy profits.

Scientists have made a groundbreaking discovery about the age of the moon, finding that it is older than previously thought. They found that the moon formed about 4.46 billion years ago, within the first 110 million years of the birth of our solar system. This research challenges previous estimates and provides valuable information about the formation of the earth-moon system. ### [Why the Lewis Model on labour and industrialisation has worked in China, not in India](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/explained-economics/why-the-lewis-model-has-worked-in-china-not-in-india-9005004/)

The article discusses the concept of industrialization in underdeveloped countries with surplus labor populations, as proposed by economist William Arthur Lewis. Lewis argued that a growing manufacturing sector could absorb the surplus labor from agriculture and other subsistence sectors by offering higher wages. However, the model has not played out as expected in India. While the share of employment in agriculture has decreased, it has not been replaced by significant growth in manufacturing. Instead, most new jobs have been created in low-paid services and construction. The movement of labor has mainly occurred within subsistence sectors rather than a shift to capitalist sectors. The exception is Gujarat, where a significant proportion of the workforce is employed in manufacturing. However, even in Gujarat, the proportion of workers in agriculture is still high compared to other states. The article suggests that the traditional model of labor transition from agriculture to industry needs to be rethought due to increasing capital-intensive manufacturing and technological advancements. NITI Aayog, an Indian government think tank, is exploring new economic development models that focus on creating remunerative jobs related to agriculture but outside the farm, such as in processing, packaging, transportation, and retailing of agricultural produce or supplying inputs and services to farmers.

### [India bans exports of some rice varieties: How this has impacted prices globally, in the country](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/explained-economics/india-bans-exports-of-some-rice-explained-9004685/)

According to an article from The Indian Express, India banned the export of non-basmati white rice in July 2023 due to depleting public stock, elevated cereal prices, and the threat of an uneven monsoon. The First Advance Estimates of major Kharif crops for 2023-24, released by the Ministry of Agriculture & Farmers’ Welfare, showed a 3.7% decrease in rice production compared to the previous year [[SOURCE 1]].

The ban on non-basmati white rice exports led to a sharp increase in rice prices across markets. Although prices have come down since then, they are still higher than the pre-ban period. The impact of India's export restrictions on rice supply, availability, and prices has been felt both domestically and globally [[SOURCE 1]].

Despite the export ban, there has been no respite from the increase in domestic rice prices. The daily average wholesale price of rice on October 24, 2023, was 27.43% higher than a month ago. The daily average retail price of rice on the same day was 12.59% higher than a year ago [[SOURCE 1]].

India is the largest exporter of rice in the world, accounting for about 40% of total rice exports in 2022. Rice exports from India are broadly categorized into basmati and non-basmati rice. While the ban applies to non-basmati white rice and broken rice, there is no ban on other sub-categories of rice [[SOURCE 1]].

In the financial year 2022-23, India exported 45.61 lakh metric tonnes of basmati rice to various countries, with Iran, Saudi Arabia, Iraq, UAE, and Yemen being the top destinations. For non-basmati rice, India exported 177.91 lakh metric tonnes, with Benin, Madagascar, and Kenya being the top buyers. The ban on non-basmati white rice and broken rice exports has significantly affected the quantity of rice shipped out and the number of countries importing from India [[SOURCE 1]].

The article also provides information on other categories of rice exports, such as par-boiled rice, husked (brown) rice, rice in husk of seed quality, and other rice in husk. It mentions the quantities exported and the top importing countries for each category [[SOURCE 1]].

In terms of rice procurement and requirement, the total production of rice during 2022-23 is estimated to be 1357.55 lakh tonnes, which is higher than the previous year's production. The government has been procuring paddy from various states for the Kharif marketing season. The total rice stock in the Central Pool as of October 1, 2023, is 221.87 lakh metric tonnes [[SOURCE 1]].

In summary, India's ban on the export of non-basmati white rice has had a significant impact on rice prices both domestically and globally. Despite the ban, domestic rice prices have continued to rise. India remains a major player in the global rice market, with significant exports of basmati and non-basmati rice.

### [An Expert Explains | 10 years of China’s Belt and Road Initiative: The project, its aims and where it stands now](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/explained-global/expert-explains-10-years-chinas-bri-xi-jinping-status-explained-9004356/)

The Belt and Road Initiative (BRI) is a decade-old infrastructure funding project initiated by China's President Xi Jinping. It aims to connect Asia, Africa, and Europe through investments worth billions of dollars. The BRI initially consisted of the Silk Road Economic 'Belt' and the sea trade infrastructure called 'Road'. Over time, it expanded to include six international Economic Corridors.

Criticism has been raised regarding the sustainability of the debts incurred by BRI projects. Some countries, such as India and the United States, have accused China of engaging in 'debt trap diplomacy' by aiming to acquire assets if loan repayment becomes impossible.

Despite the criticism, the BRI has attracted significant participation from over 150 countries and 30 international organizations. Currently, more than 3,000 BRI projects valued at $1 trillion are underway globally.

The article highlights concerns about transparency, debt traps, and sustainability of BRI projects. It also discusses the evolving perceptions of the European Union (EU) and the United States towards the initiative. While the EU initially viewed the BRI positively, growing apprehensions have led to a reassessment of the relationship with China.

The China-Pakistan Economic Corridor (CPEC) is a flagship BRI project, strengthening the strategic partnership between China and Pakistan. Despite concerns about its long-term impact on Pakistan's economy, it is unlikely that Pakistan will abandon the CPEC.

India has consistently expressed reservations about the BRI due to sovereignty-related issues and geopolitical implications. China's expanding economic presence in South Asia, including investments in ports, has concerned Indian policymakers.

Geopolitical tensions, particularly the measures taken by the US and Europe to reduce economic dependence on China, could impact the Chinese economy and the expansion of the BRI. Consequently, China may need to re-strategize some of its BRI projects.

Despite criticism, the BRI has given China diplomatic and geopolitical leverage within the Global South. Western countries are still searching for alternatives to BRI in terms of infrastructure investments.

In response to the challenging geopolitical and economic landscape, China is adjusting its ambitions and focusing on green and smaller-scale development projects. The success of other China-led initiatives, such as the Global Security Initiative and the Global Development Initiative, largely depends on the BRI's success.

The scheme aims to provide eight lakh pucca houses to the homeless with a budget of Rs 1,6320 crore. It will be implemented in three phases, targeting two lakh houses in the current financial year, 3.5 lakh houses in FY 2024-25, and 2.5 lakh houses in FY 2025-26. The scheme was needed because existing government schemes have been unable to cover all eligible beneficiaries. Despite repeated requests to the central government, these beneficiaries were not added to the list. Therefore, the state decided to create its own housing scheme. Under the AAY, beneficiaries will receive a three-room and one kitchen house with a total area of 31 sq m. The government has allocated Rs 2 lakh per beneficiary, to be disbursed in four installments. There is also a provision for beneficiaries to work under the MGNREGS and receive wages for building the house. Additionally, a toilet can be added to the house using funds from the Swaccha Bharat Mission or other available schemes. All houses built under the AAY will be registered in the name of women in the beneficiary families. The scheme will benefit people belonging to six categories, and a list of beneficiaries will be prepared based on specific criteria.

### [The centrality of natural gas in ties between India and Qatar](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/explained-economics/natural-gas-lng-india-qatar-relations-9002396/)

The article discusses the death sentence given to eight former personnel of the Indian Navy by a court in Qatar and its implications on the India-Qatar relationship. It highlights the significant trade imbalance between the two countries, with imports from Qatar outweighing India's exports. The trade between India and Qatar is heavily dominated by liquefied natural gas (LNG), which accounts for over half of India's overall import volumes of the fuel. India's import dependency on natural gas is around 50%, and with the government's aim to increase natural gas consumption, imports are expected to rise in the future. Qatar holds a significant leverage in the trade relationship due to its dominance in the LNG market.

India's largest LNG importer, Petronet LNG, has a long-term contract with Qatar for the import of 8.5 million tonnes per annum (mtpa) of LNG. India has set an ambitious target to increase the share of natural gas in its primary energy mix, which will result in further growth in LNG imports. Natural gas is seen as a cleaner alternative to conventional petroleum fuels and is cheaper than crude oil. Given India's energy security concerns and ambitions, the case of the retired navy personnel presents a sensitive challenge for Indian diplomacy.

In FY2022-23, India's total imports from Qatar were valued at $16.81 billion, with LNG imports accounting for 49.5% of the total. India's other major imports from Qatar include liquefied petroleum gas (LPG), plastics, and petrochemicals. On the other hand, India's exports to Qatar were valued at just $1.97 billion in the same period. India imported 10.74 million tonnes of LNG from Qatar, representing 54% of the total LNG imports. Despite efforts to diversify sourcing, it may take several years to reduce the reliance on Qatar for LNG imports.

The global LNG market has become a seller's market due to disruptions in Russian natural gas supplies to Europe. The spot LNG market is prone to price volatility, while term contracts provide a more stable and reasonable price. Qatar, as the world's largest exporter of LNG, holds a strong position in the market. Many countries, including India, are looking to secure long-term contracts with major suppliers like Qatar. The article also mentions that the global LNG market may eventually turn into a buyer's market due to new LNG export projects, but this is still years away.

Overall, the article highlights the significance of the India-Qatar trade relationship, especially in terms of LNG imports, and the challenges India faces in diversifying its energy sources and reducing reliance on Qatar.

### [Inform customers when banks ask for their credit score: What RBI has told credit bureaus](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/explained-economics/customers-banks-credit-score-rbi-9001711/)

The Reserve Bank of India (RBI) has instructed credit information companies (CICs) to notify customers via SMS or email when their Credit Information Report (CIR) is accessed by banks and non-banking finance companies (NBFCs). Additionally, banks and NBFCs must send alerts to customers when they submit information on default or Days Past Due (DPD) on existing credit. These new rules will be implemented within six months. CICs collect and analyze credit information from banks and NBFCs to calculate credit scores for individuals and credit ranks for companies. A high credit score increases the chances of obtaining a loan at a favorable rate, while a low score may result in loan denial. The RBI has also mandated that CICs provide individuals with a free full credit report, including credit score, once a year. Customers can apply for data correction if they believe their information is incorrect, and CICs must inform customers of the reasons for data correction rejections. CICs should conduct periodic reviews of their search and match logic algorithm and disclose complaint details on their websites.

### [What is the ‘One nation, One Student ID’ initiative of the Union govt?](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/one-nation-one-student-id-apaar-explained-9000086/)

The Indian government has introduced the Automated Permanent Academic Account Registry (APAAR), a special ID system for all students in India. Under this initiative, each student will receive a lifelong APAAR ID, which will track their academic progress from pre-primary education to higher education. APAAR will also serve as a gateway to Digilocker, a digital system where students can store important documents and achievements. The goal of APAAR is to make education hassle-free and reduce the need for physical documents. It aims to reduce fraud and duplicate certificates by providing a single, trusted reference for educational institutions. Every individual will have a unique APAAR ID linked to the Academic Bank Credit (ABC), which stores information about the credits earned by students. When a student changes schools, their data in the ABC will be transferred to the new school using the APAAR ID, eliminating the need for physical documents. To sign up for APAAR, students will need to provide basic information and their Aadhar number for verification. The government assures that the information shared will be kept confidential and only used for educational purposes. Registration for APAAR is voluntary, not mandatory.

### [Expert Explains | What happens now in Mahua Moitra cash-for-query case: process, precedent](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/p-d-t-achary-explains-mahua-case-process-precedent-8999614/)

BJP MP Nishikant Dubey and lawyer Jai Anant Dehadrai will appear before the ethics committee of Lok Sabha on Thursday in connection with Dubey's accusation against Trinamool Congress member Mahua Moitra. Dubey alleged that Moitra accepted bribes to ask questions in parliament that targeted the Adani group. The matter should have been referred to the privileges committee, which is responsible for addressing breaches of privilege and contempt of the house. The ethics committee typically deals with questions of propriety but not alleged breaches of privilege. The committee will call the parties involved, including the complainant, witnesses, and Moitra, who has the right to cross-examine the complainant. The committee can recommend suspension, but it does not have executive powers. Its recommendations will go before the house for a decision, which can accept or modify the punishment. If a member is expelled, they can challenge it based on unconstitutionality, gross illegality, or denial of natural justice. The court's intervention in such cases is limited.

### [LS Ethics Committee: its constitution, members](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/ls-ethics-committee-its-constitution-members-8999613/)

The Lok Sabha Ethics Committee, responsible for handling complaints against members of parliament, last met on July 27, 2021. The committee, which was established over twenty years ago, mostly deals with relatively minor offenses. The members of the committee are appointed by the Speaker for a one-year term, and it is currently headed by Vinod Kumar Sonkar from the BJP. Other members include representatives from various political parties. The idea of ethics panels for both houses was proposed in 1996, and the Rajya Sabha ethics committee was formed in 1997. The Lok Sabha ethics committee was recommended during the 13th Lok Sabha and became a permanent part of the house in 2015. In 2005, motions were adopted to expel several MPs accused of accepting money for asking questions in parliament. The BJP demanded that the report be sent to the privileges committee so that the MPs could defend themselves. Any person can file a complaint against an MP, accompanied by evidence and an affidavit. The committee only entertains complaints that involve misconduct by MPs and conducts a preliminary inquiry before deciding to examine a complaint. After evaluating a complaint, the committee presents its report to the Speaker for consideration. The work of the ethics committee and privileges committee often overlap, with more serious accusations typically going to the privileges committee. The privileges committee's role is to protect the freedom, authority, and dignity of parliament. The ethics committee specifically addresses cases of misconduct by MPs.

### [What is Cubism — and how Braque and Picasso invented it](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/explained-culture/what-is-cubism-braque-picasso-8999424/)

Pablo Picasso, a renowned Spanish artist, was born on October 25, 1881. He is known for his extensive body of work, which includes paintings, drawings, engravings, sculptures, and ceramics. Picasso, along with French painter George Braque, invented Cubism, a revolutionary art movement that emerged in 1907 and lasted until 1914. Cubism rejected traditional approaches to art and instead focused on depicting objects from multiple viewpoints. It was not about creating cubes, despite its name. The movement had a significant impact on the art world, inspiring other non-representational art movements like Surrealism. Picasso and Braque drew inspiration from French artist Paul Cézanne, who represented nature using geometric shapes. Cubism also reflected the scientific and technological advancements of the early 20th century, with artists discussing concepts like the fourth dimension. Cubism developed in two phases: analytical cubism, characterized by muted tones and multiple viewpoints, and synthetic cubism, which incorporated real-life objects and brighter colors.

Wed, 25 Oct 2023 14:11:10 +0000
### [The story of the Osage Indians’ murders, subject of Scorsese’s Killers of the Flower Moon](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/explained-history/osage-indians-murders-killers-of-the-flower-moon-8999410/)

The article discusses the historical events surrounding the Osage Nation, a Native American tribe in Oklahoma, during the 1920s. After oil was discovered on their land, members of the tribe were murdered, leading to the formation of the FBI. The killings exposed the exploitation of Native American resources by white settlers. The upcoming film "Killers of the Flower Moon" tells this story based on David Grann's book. The Osage were forced to give up their territory and move to Kansas before being allocated a reservation in northeastern Oklahoma. The land they moved to had significant oil deposits, which made the Osage wealthy. However, they were not fully in control of their finances due to a system of financial guardians mandated by law. The article focuses on Mollie Burkhart's family, whose members were targeted for their headrights to the oil deposits. Investigations into the murders were initially unsuccessful until an FBI agent named Tom White connected them to William K Hale. Eventually, a law was passed to prevent non-Osage individuals from inheriting headrights. Many believe that the death toll among the Osage was much higher than officially reported.

### [NCERT panel suggests replacing ‘India’ with ‘Bharat’: A brief history of the nation’s names](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/explained-history/ncert-textbooks-replace-india-with-bharat-8999272/)

The 2022 Social Science committee of the National Council of Educational Research and Training (NCERT) in India has suggested that the country should be called "Bharat" in all Social Science textbooks up to Class 12. The committee's chairman, Prof CI Issac, hopes that this change will be implemented in the next academic year, pending approval from the NCERT. The debate about the nation's name has been ongoing, and it gained attention during the G20 Summit when the invitation for the dinner hosted by the President mentioned "The President of Bharat" instead of "President of India." The name "Bharat" has roots in ancient literature and is associated with the land between the sea in the south and the abode of snow in the north. The name "Hindustan" also has historical significance, derived from the Persian cognate form of the Sanskrit word "Sindhu." Over time, the name "India" became more commonly used, especially during British colonial rule. The adoption of "India" as the primary name for the country in the Constitution of India was not without debate, with some arguing for "Bharat" or "Bharatvarsha" instead. Ultimately, the committee adopted the name "India, that is, Bharat."

### [The new made-in-India EV charging standard for bikes and scooters: here’s why it matters](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/explained-global/india-new-national-ev-charging-standard-8998587/)

The Bureau of Indian Standards (BIS) has approved a new AC and DC combined charging connector standard for light electric vehicles (LEVs) in India. This standard, called ISI7017 (Part 2 / Sec 7): 2023, is the first in the world to combine AC and DC charging for LEVs. It was developed through collaboration among NITI Aayog, the Department of Science and Technology, Ather Energy, and other government and industry stakeholders. The new standard aims to address the issue of multiple charging standards used by different EV makers in India, which makes it difficult for public charging stations to cater to all types of EVs. While the new standard is a step towards interoperability and addressing range anxiety, it does not mandate EV makers to use a uniform standard. In comparison, countries like China have a national standard (GB/T) for EV charging connectors, while Europe uses the Combined Charging System (CCS) as its predominant standard. The US does not have a national standard but some collaboration between EV makers has taken place. Japan uses the CHAdeMO charging standard.

### [The new made-in-India EV charging standard for bikes and scooters: here’s why it matters](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/explained-sci-tech/india-new-national-ev-charging-standard-8998587/)

The Bureau of Indian Standards (BIS) has approved a new charging connector standard for light electric vehicles (LEVs) in India. The standard, called ISI7017 (Part 2 / Sec 7): 2023, is the first in the world to combine alternating current (AC) and direct current (DC) charging for LEVs. This standard was developed through collaboration among NITI Aayog, the Department of Science and Technology, Ather Energy, and other stakeholders. The new standard aims to address the issue of multiple charging standards used by different EV makers in India, which makes it difficult for public charging stations to cater to all types of EVs. However, the standard is not mandatory for EV makers to use, which means that different charging standards may still be used, contributing to range anxiety and hindering the adoption of EVs. In contrast, countries like China, the United States, Europe, and Japan have their own national or regional charging connector standards in place.

### [Telangana’s law under scanner: How preventive detention works](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/explained-law/telanganas-law-under-scanner-how-preventive-detention-works-8998057/)

The Telangana government's use of its stringent preventive detention law is being questioned by the Supreme Court ahead of the Assembly polls next month. The law allows for the detention of individuals by the state without trial or conviction, based solely on suspicion. Preventive detention can last up to a year unless extended. Unlike pre-trial detention, preventive detention can be used as a measure even if no crime has been committed. India's constitution allows for preventive detention under Article 22, despite its emphasis on individual liberty. Several states in India also have similar preventive detention laws. The article explains the provisions and safeguards under Article 22 for preventive detention, including the role of advisory boards and the right of the detainee to challenge the detention order in court. However, the scope of judicial review for preventive detention is limited, as the constitution prioritizes the state's subjective satisfaction over fundamental rights. Courts often strike down detention orders on technical grounds.

### [Why Mumbai is witnessing more poor air quality days](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/explained-climate/why-mumbai-is-witnessing-more-poor-air-quality-days-8998070/)

The air quality in Mumbai during the winter months is deteriorating, similar to what has been happening in Delhi for the past decade. Previously, Mumbai was considered immune to air pollution due to its coastal location and strong sea breezes that would blow away dust and particles. However, in the last two years, this geographical advantage no longer offers reliable protection. Last year, Mumbai experienced a long period of poor air quality from November to January, with some days being more polluted than Delhi. This trend continued last week when the air quality index (AQI) exceeded 300 in certain parts of the city. Adverse meteorological conditions have contributed to these particularly bad spells, but overall, Mumbai's air quality has been consistently declining. The direction and strength of winds play a crucial role in determining air quality in Mumbai. While the city generates pollutants at a similar rate to Delhi and other urban centers, strong winds usually help sweep away dust particles. However, disruptions in wind patterns can impact air quality, as seen last year when winds changed directions less frequently than usual. This disruption may have been influenced by La Nina conditions in the eastern Pacific Ocean. La Nina is a large-scale phenomenon that affects weather events worldwide. Last year's prolonged and strong La Nina event likely played a role in Mumbai's poor winter air quality. However, La Nina has ended and been replaced by El Nino, whose effects are not yet known. Last week's dip in air quality was caused by different factors, including calmer winds as the monsoon retreated and a temperature gradient between Mumbai and nearby hilly regions that brought dust from construction projects into the city. While unfavorable local weather conditions contribute to poor air quality, the main cause is high levels of pollution exceeding the city's carrying capacity due to increased economic activity, vehicles, construction, consumption, and emissions. Experts warn that Mumbai's air quality has been steadily declining over the past decade and efforts to mitigate the impacts are insufficient.

### [How the United Nations was born on this day in 1945, and how it has evolved](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/everyday-explainers/united-nations-founded-history-1945-evolution-explained-8997530/)

Hmm, I am not sure. Email support@chatbase.co for more info.The United Nations (UN) was founded on October 24, 1945, after the end of World War II. It was created to maintain world peace and prevent future wars. The UN replaced the League of Nations, which had failed to prevent the outbreak of the Second World War. The idea for the UN was first discussed by US President Franklin D. Roosevelt and British Prime Minister Winston Churchill in 1941, and it was officially established with the signing of the UN Declaration by representatives of 51 nations in 1945. The UN has since grown to include 193 member states and has expanded its scope to address various global issues such as health, environment, and human rights. It has achieved significant milestones, including the creation of the World Health Organization (WHO) in 1948 and the establishment of the International Criminal Court in 2002. However, it has also faced criticism for its failures, such as its inability to prevent genocides in Rwanda and sexual misconduct allegations against its peacekeeping missions. The UN aims to address these challenges and work towards sustainable development goals in the next decade.

### [China to build the world’s largest ‘ghost particle’ detector: What are neutrinos and why do they need to be detected?](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/explained-sci-tech/china-largest-ghost-particle-detector-neutrinos-explained-8997219/)

China is constructing a large telescope in the western Pacific Ocean to detect neutrinos, also known as "ghost particles". Neutrinos are tiny, chargeless particles that are abundant in the universe but difficult to observe due to their weak interactions with other particles. The new telescope, called "Trident", will be the largest of its kind and will be located in the South China Sea. It is expected to be 10,000 times more sensitive than existing underwater telescopes and will help scientists study the energy and source of neutrinos. Neutrinos are still not fully understood, and their behavior defies established rules of physics. However, understanding them could help solve scientific mysteries such as the origin of cosmic rays. The construction of Trident will bring scientists closer to unraveling the secrets of neutrinos and the origins of our universe.

### [How Ramleela reached Trinidad and became a representation of ‘Indianness’](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/explained-culture/ramleela-trinidad-indentured-labour-8996443/)

Ramleela, a traditional folk theatre performance in India, has made its way to Trinidad through the Indian diaspora. In the early 19th century, Trinidad turned to indentured labor from India after slavery was abolished. The first boatload of indentured Indians arrived in Trinidad in 1845, bringing with them their culture and the Ramcharitmanas, a Hindu text. Ramleela became popular among the Indian community in Trinidad, but its popularity declined as formal education spread and younger generations became less familiar with the Manas. Migration to urban areas and exposure to other cultural influences also contributed to the decline. However, Ramleela has undergone innovation and adaptation to appeal to younger audiences, with increased financial backing leading to higher production values. Despite these changes, the essence of the story and performance remains the same, serving as an anchor for the Indian community in Trinidad and representing their Indianness.

The Election Commission of India (ECI) is responsible for the allotment of symbols, which can be either reserved or free. Recognized parties get exclusive symbols, while unregistered parties can choose from free symbols. The ECI publishes lists specifying parties and their symbols through a notification in the Gazette of India. The article also mentions that the late MS Sethi, a former draughtsman for the ECI, sketched many of the symbols used by political parties. Parties are allowed to state their preferences for symbols, but they must not resemble existing symbols or have any religious or communal connotations. When a recognized party splits, the Election Commission decides on the symbol assignment.

### [ExplainSpeaking | Bidenomics: What is it? Has it worked? What is its significance?](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/explained-economics/us-joe-biden-economics-explained-8995387/)

Hmm, I am not sure. Email support@chatbase.co for more info.In an article by Udit Misra, it is highlighted that the year 2024 will be significant for the global economy due to elections in major economies such as India, Russia, the UK, the EU, and the US. These countries collectively contribute to more than 50% of the global GDP in 2023, with a combined GDP of over $54 trillion. The US election is particularly important, as President Joe Biden is expected to run for re-election, facing a challenge from former President Donald Trump. Bidenomics, the economic policies introduced by President Biden, is a key focus of the article [[SOURCE 1]].

Bidenomics is a term used to refer to the economic policies implemented by the Biden administration. It is a reaction to the economic structure that has been in place since the Reagan presidency in 1981. Reaganomics, which aimed to stimulate economic activity through tax cuts for businesses and reduced government intervention, has been criticized for exacerbating income and wealth inequality. Bidenomics seeks to address these inequalities and promote inclusive growth by investing in infrastructure, reducing trade dependence on countries like China, and raising living standards for the middle and lower-income groups [[SOURCE 1]].

The article discusses two main concerns regarding Bidenomics. First, there is a question of whether it is working effectively. While macro indicators such as GDP growth and unemployment rates show positive trends, there are concerns about income and wealth inequality. Real income growth for the middle 40% of the working population has remained negative for most of the Biden presidency, indicating that the benefits of economic growth may not be reaching everyone [[SOURCE 1]].

The second concern is the ideological disagreement with Bidenomics. Critics argue that the policies implemented, such as providing subsidies to domestic companies, may distort markets and lead to a global subsidy war. This could harm poorer nations and increase inefficiency and corruption within the US. However, proponents of Bidenomics argue that it is necessary to address inequality and counter global rivals like China [[SOURCE 1]].

In conclusion, Bidenomics is an economic approach aimed at reducing inequality and promoting inclusive growth. While it has shown positive macroeconomic indicators, there are concerns about income and wealth inequality and ideological disagreements with the policies implemented. The impact of Bidenomics will continue to be debated as the Biden administration moves forward with its economic agenda [[SOURCE 1]].

### [What is ‘Visa Shopping’ and why is it being done to enter countries in Europe](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/visa-shopping-immigration-europe-schengen-explained-8994712/)

The practice of 'visa shopping' is becoming popular in several Indian states, including Punjab. Visa shopping refers to obtaining visas for countries that individuals may or may not visit during the granted visa period. This allows people to enter their preferred countries, particularly in Europe, even if they have obtained a visa for another European nation. People are also purchasing visas for countries they have no immediate plans to visit in order to increase their chances of obtaining visas for countries of their choice in the future. The Schengen Visa is a popular example of this, as it allows travellers to explore many European countries without the need for separate visas. The Schengen system was established in 1985 and allows people to travel freely between member countries without going through border controls. Many people opt to apply for a Schengen Visa from a country with an easier application process and then use it to travel to their intended destination. While this practice is not illegal if travellers adhere to the norms and guidelines of the visa process, legal concerns arise if a person seeks a visa for a particular country but stays in another or shows that their intentions were not to stay in that country but to only use it as an entry point. The Embassy of Estonia in New Delhi has advised against visa shopping and warned that there may be severe consequences, including refusal of the visa or being offloaded from the plane or returned back home from the EU borders. Repeatedly engaging in visa shopping may also raise suspicion among officials.

Sat, 21 Oct 2023 12:44:15 +0000

The article discusses the role of Bedouin soldiers in Israel's military and their contributions to the country. The Bedouins are a nomadic Muslim Arab people who traditionally lived in the Negev desert in southern Israel. In the past, they provided security and intelligence to early Jewish settlers in Palestine during the Arab-Israeli War. Israel recognized a large number of Bedouins as citizens in the 1950s and built settlements for them in the Negev. Many Bedouin soldiers serve in the Israeli Defense Forces (IDF), particularly in scouting and tracking units. Specialized search and rescue units, mainly comprising Bedouins, were also formed by the IDF. The article mentions that army training is not compulsory for Bedouin youth, but many still volunteer, with a record number of approximately 600 enlisting in 2021. The Bedouin population in Israel currently numbers around 210,000 individuals, with a significant presence in the Negev desert. The Israeli government has launched Operation Negev Shield to integrate Bedouin communities into society through educational programs. The article also highlights the appointment of Israel's first Bedouin ambassador and the presence of Bedouin entrepreneurs in the country's high-tech sector.

Email support@chatbase.co for more info.The India-Middle East-Europe Economic Corridor (IMEC) faces significant challenges and complexities in light of the recent fighting in Israel and escalating tensions in the region. The project aims to physically connect a vast stretch of the Eurasian subcontinent through cross-country railway lines and shipping corridors. However, officials from multiple government ministries indicate that the ongoing conflict has made progress on the project even more difficult.

When Prime Minister Modi announced the IMEC alongside US President Joe Biden and Saudi Arabia's Crown Prince Mohammed bin Salman, there was an acknowledgment of the logistical complexities involved. This includes potential customs checks and clearance for consignments as well as navigating the complex geopolitics of the West Asian region.

The design of the corridor involves traversing diverse nations with different political dynamics, strategic interests, and historical tensions. The US backing was seen as crucial, especially considering the involvement of countries like Israel, Jordan, and Saudi Arabia. However, the recent outbreak of fighting between Hamas and Israel threatens to disrupt these efforts, potentially affecting the Saudi-Israel peace initiative.

The fresh trouble in the West Asia region poses a risk to diplomatic outreach between Israel and Saudi Arabia, as well as other countries like the UAE, Bahrain, and Jordan. Moreover, Turkey has announced its own project as a counter to the IMEC, citing disputed waters along the proposed sea route between Haifa in Israel and Piraeus in Greece.

The IMEC is part of a US-led initiative called the Partnership for Global Infrastructure Investment (PGII), with discussions starting in January 2023. The alignment of the corridor stretches from Western India to Europe via the ports of Piraeus, Haifa, and Dubai. The corridor includes two separate routes: the east corridor connecting India to the Arabian Gulf and the northern corridor connecting the Arabian Gulf to Europe.

The IMEC aims to establish a reliable and cost-effective cross-border ship-to-rail transit network to facilitate the movement of goods and services between India and Europe. It also presents opportunities for laying cables for electricity and digital connectivity, as well as a pipeline for clean hydrogen export. However, the current conflict and geopolitical complexities pose significant challenges to the realization of this ambitious project.

### [Israel-Palestine conflict: How international humanitarian law views forced displacement and human shields](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/explained-law/international-humanitarian-law-forced-displacement-human-shields-8992746/)

The use of human shields is considered a war crime under international humanitarian law (IHL), and it is also prohibited to target human shields. The article discusses the complexities of the situation in Gaza due to the population density and highlights that regardless of intent, it would be illegal for Israel to attack civilian homes used for military purposes. Recently, Israel ordered hundreds of thousands of civilians to evacuate from northern Gaza, which could constitute forcible displacement under the statute of the International Criminal Court. The order was criticized by organizations such as the International Committee of the Red Cross and the Norwegian Refugee Council. While warning civilians of military operations is necessary, it is illegal to threaten people to leave. Any displacement of civilians must be temporary and for their benefit, with guarantees of safety and return.

### [Canada visa services now available only in Delhi: How will Indian visa seekers, including students, be impacted?](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/everyday-explainers/canada-visa-services-delhi-india-students-impact-explained-8992540/)

Canada has suspended visa and consular services at its consulates in Chandigarh, Mumbai, and Bengaluru, and will now only offer these services at the Canadian High Commission in New Delhi. This decision comes after Canada withdrew 41 diplomats from India, following New Delhi's decision to revoke their diplomatic immunity. The ongoing diplomatic dispute between the two countries was triggered by Prime Minister Justin Trudeau's allegation that India may have been involved in the killing of a Khalistani separatist in Canada. 

The expected fallout of this situation is that there will be a significant slowdown in Indian visa applications for Canada. A large portion of these applications come from Indian students who wish to study in Canadian universities. Currently, there are over 319,000 registered Indian students in Canada, making it the second-largest destination for Indian students after the United States. This downsizing of Canada's diplomatic corps and the suspension of visa services in major Indian cities will impact the processing of these applications, potentially causing a backlog.

Indian students make a substantial contribution to the Canadian economy through tuition fees and their spending on living expenses. Tuition fees for international students in Canada are higher than those for domestic students, and it is estimated that Indian students contribute approximately Canadian $10 billion annually to the education sector in the country. Moreover, international students, including Indian students, fill essential jobs in critical sectors of the Canadian economy.

While Immigration, Refugees and Citizenship Canada (IRCC) has stated that it is reducing the number of employees in India from 27 to 5, the visa application centers operated by third-party contractors will not be impacted. These centers process the majority of visa applications outside the country, through IRCC's global network. The remaining staff will focus on urgent cases, visa printing, and working with the visa application centers.

Overall, the slowdown in the visa process is expected to have negative consequences for Indian students and the Canadian economy. The diplomatic dispute between Canada and India has further strained relations and created challenges for those seeking to travel and study between the two countries.

### [The colonial — and anti-colonial — roots of Durga Puja](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/explained-history/history-durga-puja-bengal-company-8992370/)

Hmm, I am not sure. Email support@chatbase.co for more info.The article discusses the history of Durga Puja, a religious festival celebrated in Bengal, India. The origins of modern Durga Puja can be traced back to the nexus between Bengali zamindars (landowners) and merchants, and the East India Company during colonial rule. With Company rule came economic opportunities, leading to the rise of a new class of powerful natives who used Durga Puja as an occasion to display their wealth and socialize with the British elites. By the late 19th century, Durga Puja became a part of the nascent nationalist project, symbolizing the nation and its fight against foreign rule. After Lord Curzon's decision to Partition Bengal in 1905, Durga Pujas became places where collective consciousness and action were forged in the Swadeshi Movement. In the 1920s, public Pujas began to emerge, making the festival accessible to everyone regardless of caste or residence.

### [PM Modi flags off RRTS: What is this mass transport system, how it can benefit NCR](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/pm-modi-to-flag-off-rrts-what-is-this-mass-transport-system-how-it-can-benefit-ncr-8991437/)

Prime Minister Narendra Modi has inaugurated the first leg of India's Regional Rapid Transit System (RRTS), which is the country's first mass rapid system dedicated to regional connectivity. The trains on this first section have the capability to run at speeds up to 180 km/h, which will eventually reduce the journey time between Delhi and Meerut to less than an hour. The RRTS is an integrated mass transit network aimed at achieving balanced and sustainable urban development by providing better connectivity and access across the National Capital Region (NCR). The idea for this network originated from a study commissioned by the Indian Railways in 1998-99, which identified the possibility of connecting various locations in the NCR through fast commuter trains. The proposal was later re-examined in 2006 with the extension of the Delhi Metro lines to towns in the NCR. The National Capital Region Transport Corporation (NCRTC) was established to implement the RRTS project, which includes eight corridors to connect different towns in the NCR. The RRTS, also known as Namo Bharat, aims to unlock the full potential of the NCR by enhancing multi-modal connectivity and reducing congestion on roads, highways, metro, and railway networks. Additionally, the project is expected to boost employment and stimulate economic activity in the region. The RRTS trains will run faster than metro trains, operating at speeds up to 180 km/h, compared to the maximum speed of 100-120 km/h for Delhi Metro trains. The Phase I development includes three corridors: Delhi-Ghaziabad-Meerut (82 km), Delhi-Gurugram-SNB-Alwar (164 km), and Delhi-Panipat (103 km). The project plans to develop five more corridors in the future. The Sarai Kale Khan station in Delhi will serve as the central hub for all three Phase I corridors. The priority section of the Delhi-Ghaziabad-Meerut corridor, a 17 km stretch with five stations, has been inaugurated, and the entire corridor is scheduled to be operational by 2025.

The Indian Space Research Organisation (ISRO) is set to conduct the Flight Test Vehicle Abort Mission-1 (TV-D1) on Saturday, as part of its preparations for launching an Indian astronaut into space. The TV-D1 mission will test the Crew Escape System of the Gaganyaan project, which allows the crew to leave the spacecraft in an emergency. The rocket will reach an altitude of almost 17 km before triggering an abort signal, leading to the separation of the crew module and its descent using a parachute. The test mission will last 532 seconds and will demonstrate the new Test Vehicle and a basic version of the crew module. ISRO has developed a low-cost Test Vehicle specifically for these tests, using existing liquid propulsion technology. The Gaganyaan human space flight programme has a budget of around Rs 9,000 crore ($1.2 billion).

### [Vladimir Putin seen with ‘nuclear briefcase’ in China: Its history, what it contains](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/everyday-explainers/vladimir-putin-nuclear-briefcase-explained-8991091/)

During Russian President Vladimir Putin's recent visit to China, he was seen with officers carrying the "nuclear briefcase." This briefcase is part of a system for authorizing the launch of nuclear strikes. It is used to convey orders for a nuclear strike to the rocket forces of the country. The briefcases are also carried by the defense minister and the chief of general staff, who coordinate with the president in case of a potential order to strike. The first known instance of a nuclear briefcase being used was in 1995 during a crisis. The briefcases were developed during the Cold War to cut down reaction time in the event of a sudden attack. The American president also has a similar briefcase, officially called the presidential emergency satchel or the "football," which contains information on retaliatory options and authentication codes to authorize a nuclear attack. Other countries have similar systems as well. There have been instances of misplaced or lost codes in the past.

The article discusses the oversimplification of the Palestinian identity in media coverage of the Israel-Palestine conflict. It highlights that the emphasis is often placed on Palestinians being Muslims who are in conflict with Jewish Israelis, which fails to acknowledge the rich and complex history of the Palestinians. The term "Palestine" originated in ancient Greece and referred to the coastal land between Phoenicia and Egypt. The region underwent Islamisation and saw the emergence of various overlapping identities. After the defeat of the Ottomans in World War I, Palestine was divided among the British and the French. The Palestinian national charter of 1968 emphasized the Palestinians' Arab identity but did not define Palestine in religious terms. The majority of Palestinians today are Sunni Muslims, but there is also a Jewish minority in Palestinian areas. Palestinian Christians make up a small percentage of the population.

A new Banksy mural has appeared near the Edgware Road Underground Station in London. The artwork, which depicts robotic arms being pulled by three individuals, bears the words "another world is possible." While Banksy has not claimed ownership of the piece, it has attracted a large number of visitors. Interpretations of the artwork vary, with some viewing it as a warning against the dangers of artificial intelligence and others seeing it as a commentary on the ongoing Israel-Palestine conflict.

Banksy has a history of working in Palestine since the early 2000s, with his artworks being spotted in the region, including on the West Bank wall. He also owns a hotel next to the separation wall in Bethlehem called the Walled Off Hotel. The hotel features rooms with artwork by Banksy and other artists, a gallery showcasing Palestinian artworks, and a museum with audio-visual presentations about the region's history.

One of Banksy's most notable statements on the Israel-Palestine conflict is a video he released in 2015, sarcastically promoting tourism in Gaza. The video highlights the devastation in Gaza and the restrictions faced by its residents, juxtaposed with the Israeli military presence. Banksy also references the destruction caused by the 2014 military operation and the network of illegal tunnels. The video concludes with a quote spray-painted on a wall, emphasizing the importance of not remaining neutral in conflicts between the powerful and the powerless.

Banksy's artworks in the region include the famous "Love is in the Air" mural in Jerusalem, which depicts a man throwing a bouquet of flowers instead of a weapon, symbolizing peace. He has also created murals featuring children interacting with Israeli soldiers and commenting on the Israeli occupation. One of his well-known projects in Palestine is a series of large murals painted on the segregation wall in 2005, including images of children playing on a sandcastle and a variation of Banksy's "Girl with Balloon" painting.

Overall, Banksy's artworks in Palestine and his recent mural in London continue his exploration of political and social issues, provoking thought and sparking conversations about topics such as artificial intelligence and the Israel-Palestine conflict.

### [Severe drought grips the Amazon rainforest: The impact, cause and grim future](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/explained-climate/drought-amazon-rainforest-8989382/)

The Amazon rainforest is experiencing a severe drought, causing rivers to dry up and leaving Indigenous communities without water, food, or medicine. The Rio Negro, one of the largest rivers in the world, has reached a record low level. The drought has also led to dead fish and river dolphins contaminating the water supply, leaving residents with limited access to clean water. The lack of water has affected a major hydropower dam and stranded tens of thousands of people in remote villages. The dry conditions have also increased the risk of wildfires, with the Amazonas state experiencing a high number of blazes this month. The drought is attributed to the combined effects of El Nino and high sea surface temperatures. Studies suggest that with rising global temperatures, the Amazon will experience more frequent and longer droughts, potentially leading to irreversible damage and releasing stored carbon into the atmosphere. Experts emphasize the need to curb deforestation and greenhouse gas emissions to protect the Amazon and its vital role in global ecosystems.

### [Israel using white phosphorus in Gaza? 5 things about the controversial weapon](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/israel-white-phosphorus-gaza-5-things-8988938/)

Amnesty International and Human Rights Watch have accused the Israel Defense Forces (IDF) of using white phosphorus munitions in Gaza and Lebanon, in violation of International Humanitarian Law (IHL). The organizations claim that Israel's use of these munitions in densely populated areas poses long-term risks to civilians. White phosphorus is a pyrophoric substance that ignites when exposed to oxygen, producing thick smoke and intense heat. It is primarily used as a smokescreen to hide troop movement and can also be used as an incendiary weapon. Exposure to white phosphorus can cause severe burns, respiratory damage, and harm to internal organs. The use of white phosphorus munitions is regulated under the Convention on Conventional Weapons (CCW), specifically Protocol III, but there are loopholes in the regulations. Efforts have been made to strengthen the CCW to address these loopholes.

### [Supreme Court’s marriage equality judgment unpacked: Two views on four key issues](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/explained-law/scs-marriage-equality-judgment-unpacked-two-views-on-four-key-issues-8988049/)

A five-judge Constitution Bench of the Supreme Court, led by Chief Justice of India (CJI) D Y Chandrachud, has declined to grant legal status to same-sex marriages. The majority opinion, held by three judges, stated that the issue should be addressed by the legislature rather than the judiciary. While the CJI and Justice Sanjay Kishan Kaul recognized civil unions for queer couples, they were in the minority. The judges provided their views on four key questions raised during the case.

Regarding the argument that there exists a fundamental right to marry a person of one's own choice under the Constitution, the minority view disagreed. They stated that marriage may have gained significance because of state regulation, rather than being inherently fundamental. The majority opinion agreed with this perspective, emphasizing that personal preference and social status are important aspects of marriage. They also highlighted that the US courts' reasoning for declaring the right to marry a fundamental right may not be applicable.

The court considered a gender-neutral interpretation of the legislation governing civil marriages, where the state sanctions the marriage instead of religion. However, the minority view warned against entering the domain of the legislature, while the majority view stated that the legislation is intended only for heterosexual couples belonging to different faiths.

The petitioners also argued against discriminatory adoption guidelines set by the Central Adoption Resource Authority (CARA). The minority view found certain CARA regulations to be precluding unmarried couples from adopting without sufficient justification. They emphasized that good or bad parenting should not be assumed based on individuals' sexuality. The majority view agreed with the discriminatory impact on queer couples but stated that legislative changes are necessary to address this issue.

Regarding the recognition of civil unions for queer couples, the minority view located the right to form intimate associations within the fundamental right to freedom of speech and expression. They suggested that a committee would determine the rights available to queer couples in unions. In contrast, the majority view disagreed with prescribing civil unions as a choice for queer couples, suggesting that the state should facilitate this choice if the community desires it.

Overall, the Supreme Court's Constitution Bench refused to grant legal status to same-sex marriages, emphasizing that the issue falls within the domain of the legislature rather than the judiciary. The judges provided differing opinions on various aspects related to fundamental rights, legislation interpretation, adoption guidelines, and civil unions for queer couples.

### [Why Supreme Court’s same-sex marriage verdict opens no doors for queer people](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/explained-law/why-scs-same-sex-marriage-verdict-opens-no-doors-for-queer-people-8987899/)

In November 2022, two same-sex couples brought a case to the Supreme Court of India, arguing that their inability to marry under Indian family law violated their fundamental rights. After a 10-day hearing, the court reserved its judgment and delivered its final verdict on October 17, 2023.

The court, with a majority of three judges, addressed key questions in their separate opinions. The main issue was whether same-sex couples have the right to marry under the Indian Constitution and if the prevention of such marriages is discriminatory. The court unanimously answered both questions in the negative.

The court held that marriage is an institution established by law, and same-sex couples do not have the right to participate in it unless the law permits them to do so. The court found that the Special Marriage Act, which permits marriages only between a man and a woman, was constitutional.

The court also rejected the idea of allowing queer couples to enter into a civil union, stating that only an elected legislature has the authority to make such changes. The court acknowledged the complexity of aligning legal frameworks based on heterosexual unions with the right of queer individuals to marry.

Regarding adoption, since same-sex couples cannot legally marry in India, they cannot adopt as a couple. However, a single person within a same-sex couple could adopt a child. The court shifted the burden to the executive branch, encouraging it to reconsider adoption laws in line with the best interests of children.

In summary, the court's judgment did not recognize queer marriages or civil unions. It affirmed the right of queer individuals to choose a partner, even if it does not amount to a marriage or union. The court acknowledged the exclusion of the queer community from family laws but shifted the responsibility to the legislature to address these issues. The court directed the government to set up a committee to examine potential amendments to make the benefits of marriage available to same-sex couples.

The judgment represents a setback for the queer community, and the authors suggest that the legislature should take the lead in re-evaluating and improving Indian family law to be more inclusive and non-discriminatory.

Hmm, I am not sure. Email support@chatbase.co for more info.The Dutch cricket team has made history by defeating South Africa in a rain-truncated game in Dharamshala. The first written reference of the game being played in the Netherlands dates back to 1845. World War I brought an influx of talent to Dutch leagues, and cricket continued to grow in popularity throughout the 1930s. Despite World War II, cricket did not stop being played in the Netherlands and was seen as a demonstration of defiance against Nazi occupation. The professionalization of Dutch football in 1954 made it more accessible than cricket, leading to its decline as a popular sport. Additionally, the exclusionary policies of the International Cricket Council (ICC), which only granted Test status to England, Australia, and South Africa, further hindered the development of cricket in the Netherlands. If ICC had been more inclusive, Netherlands could have enjoyed regular international matches and seen an increase in standards and performances.

### [SC verdict on same sex marriages Explained Highlights: No fundamental right of same-sex couples to marry, says Supreme Court](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/explained-law/sc-verdict-on-same-sex-marriages-explained-live-8986361/)

The Supreme Court of India, headed by Chief Justice DY Chandrachud, ruled unanimously against legalizing same-sex marriage in India. The court also ruled against civil unions for non-heterosexual couples in a 3:2 verdict. The Chief Justice stated that it is up to the Parliament to decide on the issue and that the Special Marriage Act cannot be struck down or interpreted differently. The focus of the petitions filed was on a gender-neutral interpretation of the Act. Justice Kaul supported the idea of civil unions for non-heterosexual couples, while all five judges agreed that there is no fundamental right to marry and that the decision should be left to the legislature. The court heard arguments for 10 days, covering topics such as equality, privacy, marriage privileges, and the impact on children. The Center, the national child rights body, and an Islamic scholars' body opposed the petitioners. The court directed the Center to ensure non-discrimination and equal access to goods and services for the queer community, as well as raise public awareness of queer rights and establish support systems. The court also issued guidelines to the police to prevent harassment of queer individuals. The Chief Justice emphasized that marriage is not a stagnant institution but stated that the court cannot change the provisions of the Special Marriage Act. Four judgments were delivered, with each judge expressing their opinion. The Chief Justice disagreed with the Center's argument that only Parliament can decide on same-sex marriage, stating that fundamental rights must be enforced. The court also referred to previous judgments that recognized LGBTQ rights, such as the 2018 ruling that decriminalized homosexuality. Same-sex marriage is recognized in 32 countries worldwide, with most countries introducing it through legislation. The hearings were live-streamed for the public, and arguments were made on the implicit right to marry for non-heterosexual couples based on equality, non-discrimination, and the right to privacy. The inclusion of LGBTQIA+ couples in marriage acts, minimum marriageable age, and the impact on children were also discussed during the hearings.

### [Before SC, question of law in Muslim women’s right to divorce](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/explained-law/before-sc-question-of-law-in-muslim-womens-right-to-divorce-8986022/)

The Supreme Court will review a ruling by the Kerala High Court that affirmed a Muslim woman's right to pronounce extrajudicial divorce through "khula." Khula is the right of a Muslim woman to unilaterally divorce her husband, similar to talaq for Muslim men. Scholars differ on whether the husband's consent is necessary for khula. Other forms of extrajudicial divorce available to Muslim women include talaq-e-tafwiz (contract-based divorce), mubara'at (separation by mutual consent), and faskh (divorce through court intervention). The 1939 Dissolution of Muslim Marriages Act clarified and consolidated the provisions of extrajudicial divorce for Muslim women, including the faskh route. The Kerala HC ruled in 2021 that Muslim women retain their right to extrajudicial divorce under the 1939 Act.

### [Why Putin has compared Israel’s actions in Gaza to the Nazi siege of Leningrad](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/explained-history/putin-israel-gaza-nazi-siege-leningrad-8983106/)

Russian President Vladimir Putin recently warned Israel against besieging Gaza in a manner reminiscent of Nazi Germany's siege of Leningrad. The siege of Leningrad, which occurred between 1941 and 1944, resulted in the deaths of around 1.5 million civilians, leading some scholars to describe it as a genocide due to the deliberate starvation and destruction of the city's population. Operation Barbarossa, Hitler's attack on the Soviet Union, included the devastating siege of Leningrad. The city was strategically and symbolically important to the Soviets, who mobilized citizens and soldiers to defend it. The siege lasted for 872 days and involved constant shelling, famine, and harsh weather conditions. Approximately 650,000 residents of Leningrad died in 1942 alone, with around 100,000 perishing each month during the peak winter. Ultimately, 1.5 million people lost their lives during the siege, with an additional 500,000 dying during evacuation attempts. The tragedy of Leningrad was on a scale greater than the combined casualties of the United States and Britain in the entire war. German forces targeted civilians by cutting off supplies, leading to widespread starvation. Some desperate individuals even resorted to cannibalism. Putin's personal connection to the siege, as his brother died during that time, likely contributes to his invocation of this historical event. His mother also experienced near-starvation during the siege.

### [What the 2023 Nobel Economics Prize winner says on women’s aspirations, role in workforce and pay gap](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/economics-laureates-book-charts-womens-aspirations-role-in-workforce-8982036/)

Claudia Goldin, an economist and winner of the Nobel Prize in economics, explores the history of college-educated women in the United States in her book "Career & Family: Women’s Century-Long Journey Toward Equity." She examines how women's aspirations and circumstances have changed over time, highlighting the ongoing problem of women feeling short-changed in their careers compared to their husbands and male colleagues. Goldin attributes this problem to sex discrimination, gender bias, and the glass ceiling. However, she suggests that even if these issues were to disappear, the problem would still persist due to the underlying system at play.

The book categorizes women who graduated from college into five groups ranging from the 1900s to the 1990s. Goldin explores how their aspirations shifted over time, from having to choose between a career and family, to sacrificing one for the other, to ultimately aspiring for both equally. She attributes these shifts to factors such as laws that initially prohibited women and married women from working, which later changed with increased job demand after the Great Depression and the "baby boom" era following World War II.

The introduction of the birth control pill in 1960 allowed women to delay childbirth and marriage, leading to greater enrollment in post-graduate courses and aspirations for long-term careers rather than just jobs. The feminist movement of the 1960s and 1970s also played a role in increasing women's participation in the workforce, contributing significantly to economic growth.

Goldin emphasizes the tension between work and caregiving responsibilities as a major challenge faced by women today. This challenge impacts women's career goals and contributes to the gender pay gap. While previous explanations attributed the pay gap to differences in fields of study and occupational choices, Goldin argues that it expands as men and women age, get married, and have children.

The root of the problem, according to Goldin, is the prevalence of "greedy work," referring to careers that demand extensive time and attention, such as doctors, lawyers, and senior corporate executives. Greedy jobs heavily reward individuals who are available at all times, creating an imbalance between work and family life. Goldin asserts that addressing this issue requires changing the existing work and care structures, which she considers relics of a time when men were the only ones who could have both careers and families.

One part of the solution involves making rewarding, well-paying work more flexible in terms of hours and allowing for time-offs. Goldin cites fields like veterinary medicine in the US as examples of how this flexibility has been implemented to some extent. Remote work, which became more prevalent during the pandemic, also provides a potential solution.

Men play a crucial role in achieving gender equality at work and in caregiving. Goldin emphasizes the importance of men supporting their male colleagues on parental leave, advocating for public policies that subsidize childcare, and pressuring their firms to adopt more flexible practices. Ultimately, Goldin calls for a reimagination of work and care to allow both men and women to have fulfilling careers and family lives.

Sat, 14 Oct 2023 01:37:41 +0000

### [What war crimes laws apply to the Israel-Palestinian conflict?](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/explained-global/what-war-crimes-laws-apply-to-the-israel-palestinian-conflict-8980317/)

Hmm, I am not sure. Email support@chatbase.co for more info.The recent conflict between Israel and Palestinian forces, sparked by the assault from the militant group Hamas, has resulted in a high number of casualties on both sides. The conflict falls under an international system of justice that has evolved since World War II. The rules governing this conflict are based on the internationally accepted principles of armed conflict, as outlined in the 1949 Geneva Conventions. These conventions have been ratified by all UN member states and have been supplemented by rulings from international war crimes tribunals. These rules, collectively known as the "law of armed conflict" or "international humanitarian law," apply to government forces, organized armed groups, and Hamas militants.

If alleged perpetrators of atrocities in Israel, including Palestinians, or alleged crimes in the occupied Palestinian territories are not brought to justice locally, the International Criminal Court (ICC) in The Hague is the only international legal body that can bring charges. While domestic courts can exercise universal jurisdiction in war crimes cases, its scope is limited. The ICC derives its legal authority from the Rome Statute and can investigate alleged crimes on the territories of its member states or committed by their nationals when domestic authorities are "unwilling or unable" to do so. The ICC's Office of the Prosecutor has confirmed that its mandate applies to potential crimes committed in the current conflict and is currently collecting information.

The ICC, established in 2002, is the world's permanent war crimes tribunal. It has jurisdiction over war crimes, crimes against humanity, and genocide in its 123 member states or committed by their nationals. However, major powers such as China, the United States, Russia, India, and Egypt are not members. While the ICC recognizes Palestine as a member state, Israel rejects the court's jurisdiction and does not engage with it formally. Due to budget and staff constraints, ICC prosecutors are already investigating 17 cases in different countries, and the budget for investigations in the Palestinian territories for 2023 is just under a million euros ($1.06 million).

The ICC has been conducting an ongoing investigation into allegations of war crimes and crimes against humanity in the occupied Palestinian territories since 2021 but has not yet issued any arrest warrants. Prosecutors have stated that there is a reasonable basis to believe that violations have been committed by all sides, including Israeli troops, Hamas militants, and other armed Palestinian groups.

Acts that could potentially violate war crimes law include the deliberate targeting of civilians, indiscriminate rocket attacks, taking civilians as hostages by Palestinian armed groups, and the Israeli counter-strikes in Gaza resulting in the deaths of hundreds of Palestinians. The deliberate killings of civilians, hostage-taking, and collective punishment are deemed heinous crimes by human rights organizations, with no justification. Violations such as the taking of hostages, murder, and torture are explicitly prohibited under the Geneva Conventions. Israel's response to the conflict could also be subject to a war crimes investigation, particularly concerning the announcement of a tightened blockade and the possibility of a ground invasion to destroy Hamas.

US President Joe Biden has stated that Israel has the right and duty to respond but emphasized the importance of acting according to the rule of law. A siege can be considered a war crime if it targets civilians instead of being a legitimate means to undermine Hamas' military capabilities or if it is deemed disproportionate. Under international law, attacks on military objectives must be proportional and should not lead to excessive loss of civilian life or damage to civilian objects compared to the expected military advantage. A defense lawyer at the ICC highlighted the gruesome killings by Hamas militants, such as the attack on a dance rave and civilians in kibbutz communities near the Gaza border, as potential focuses for a war crimes case.

Negotiations between Sri Lanka and countries including Japan, India, and France for a debt rework deal did not go through in September 2023. The agreement with China's EXIM Bank will help Sri Lanka secure the release of the second tranche of about $334 million from the IMF and provide fiscal space for implementing reform measures.
