### [Large ozone hole detected over Antarctica: Is it a matter of concern?](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/explained-climate/large-ozone-hole-antarctica-explained-8979396/)

Hmm, I am not sure. Email support@chatbase.co for more info.Satellite measurements taken by the European Space Agency Copernicus Sentinel-5P satellite have detected a large hole in the ozone layer over Antarctica. The hole, which is referred to as an "ozone-depleted area," is 26 million square kilometers in size, approximately three times the size of Brazil. This is one of the largest ozone holes ever observed. The ozone layer acts as a protective shield against ultraviolet radiation, reducing the risk of skin cancer. The size of the ozone hole over Antarctica varies each year, opening in August and closing in November or December. This year's large ozone hole is believed to be a result of volcanic eruptions in Tonga, which released water vapor and other elements that can deplete ozone. However, human activities, particularly the use of chlorofluorocarbons, were responsible for creating significant ozone holes in the past. The Montreal Protocol was established in 1987 to phase out the production of ozone-depleting substances, which has led to a reduction in ozone holes. While ozone depletion is not a primary cause of global climate change, there are indications that rising global temperatures could impact ozone holes. Additionally, ozone holes may contribute to cooling effects and alter the progression of seasons.

### [In law change: a push for electric vehicles, less dependence on battery imports](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/explained-economics/how-new-royalty-rates-for-strategic-minerals-lithium-rees-can-help-cut-their-imports-8978912/)

The move is aimed at encouraging domestic mining to reduce imports of these minerals and promote the development of industries such as electric vehicles (EVs) and energy storage solutions. The lower royalty rates, set at 3% for lithium and niobium and 1% for REEs, align India's rates with global benchmarks and will help attract bidders to the auctions.

### [Hamas, Fatah, Islamic Jihad, PLO, and Palestinian Authority: A brief introduction to the political players in Palestine](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/explained-global/hamas-fatah-plo-palestinian-authority-islamic-jihad-israel-palestine-8976870/)

Hmm, I am not sure. Email support@chatbase.co for more info.Hmm, I am not sure. Email support@chatbase.co for more info.The recent attack on Israel has brought attention not only to Hamas but also to other significant players in Palestine, such as Fatah, Palestinian Islamic Jihad (PIJ), Palestine Liberation Organisation (PLO), and Palestinian Authority (PA). Let's take a quick look at each of them and their roles in the Israel-Palestine conflict.

Fatah, meaning "to conquer," was formed in the late 1950s in Kuwait. It was established by key figures like Yasser Arafat and Mahmoud Abbas with the objective of armed struggle against Israel to liberate Palestine. Fatah's military operations began in 1965 and were carried out from Jordan and Lebanon. In 1968, Fatah became part of the Palestine Liberation Organisation (PLO), which aimed to achieve statehood for Palestine through armed resistance. However, Fatah later transformed its approach and started negotiating with Israel. In the 1990s, Fatah-led PLO renounced armed resistance and signed the Oslo Accords, which established the Palestinian National Authority (PNA) or Palestinian Authority (PA) as an interim self-governing body leading to an independent Palestinian state. Currently, Fatah heads the PA, governing about 40% of the occupied West Bank.

Hamas, founded in the late 1980s, is another major political party in Palestine known for its ongoing armed struggle against Israel. Like Fatah, Hamas aims to establish a Palestinian state on the borders of 1967. However, unlike Fatah, Hamas has not recognized the statehood of Israel. Since 2006, Hamas has been ruling the Gaza Strip.

The Palestinian Islamic Jihad (PIJ) is the second-largest militant group in Palestine, aiming to destroy Israel and establish an Islamic Palestinian state through force. It was established in 1981 by members of Egypt's Muslim Brotherhood and is believed to receive financial support from Iran. Though Hamas and PIJ are allies, they have separate identities and some differences. PIJ has mainly focused on armed struggle but also participated in student politics and legislative elections.

The Palestine Liberation Organisation (PLO) was conceived in 1964 with the goal of liberating Palestine through armed struggle. It is a coalition of smaller Arab groups, with Fatah being the dominant one. PLO was recognized by the Arab League and the United Nations as the "sole legitimate representative of the Palestinian people." In the early 1990s, PLO gave up armed struggle and recognized the statehood of Israel, which led to the rise of Hamas.

The Palestinian Authority (PA) was founded in 1994 as an interim body to govern parts of Gaza and the West Bank. It functions as an agency of the PLO and is led by a directly-elected president, currently Mahmoud Abbas. In 2006, Hamas won the legislative elections and took control of the Gaza Strip, while the PA continued to govern parts of the West Bank.

These various groups and organizations play significant roles in the complex Israel-Palestine conflict, each with its own history, objectives, and approach.

Wed, 11 Oct 2023 01:30:05 +0000
### [What the hottest September ever reveals about climate change](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/explained-climate/what-the-hottest-september-ever-reveals-about-climate-change-8977093/)

September 2023 has been confirmed as the hottest September on record, continuing the trend of record-breaking temperatures seen throughout this year. Scientists had predicted that 2023 would be exceptionally warm due to the development of El Nino in the Pacific Ocean. The remaining three months of the year are also expected to be warmer than usual. July 2023 was not only the warmest July ever recorded, but also the warmest month overall. June, February, March, April, and May all experienced average temperatures that ranked among the highest ever for those respective months. The period between June and August was the warmest on record globally. September 2023 stood out by being significantly warmer than both pre-industrial times and a more contemporary baseline. The Secretary-General of the World Meteorology Organisation expressed concern about the ongoing warming trend and its impacts on the environment and society. It is anticipated that 2023 will surpass 2016 as the warmest year on record, with fears that it may breach the 1.5 degree Celsius mark for the first time. Despite these alarming trends, there has been little policy response from countries to address climate change. Only a significant global disruption could make a noticeable difference in reducing temperatures in the short term. The world is likely to exceed the 1.5 degree Celsius target within four years if current trends continue. The promise to triple renewable energy capacity by 2030, made at a recent G20 meeting, is considered insufficient to mitigate greenhouse gas emissions adequately. At an upcoming climate change meeting in Dubai, countries will assess global climate action and its adequacy in relation to temperature goals. It is hoped that this assessment will prompt more energetic climate action from nations.

### [SC to hear petition challenging electoral bonds: What is the scheme and its criticisms?](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/everyday-explainers/sc-petition-challenging-electoral-bonds-explained-criticism-8976353/)

The Supreme Court has announced that it will hear petitions challenging India's electoral bonds scheme on October 31. Introduced in 2018, electoral bonds are interest-free bearer instruments that allow anonymous donations to political parties. They can be purchased in various denominations from authorized branches of the State Bank of India. Political parties have 15 days to encash the bonds and use the funds for electoral expenses. If the bonds are not encashed within the timeframe, the money is deposited into the Prime Minister's Relief Fund. The scheme has faced legal challenges, with critics arguing that it lacks transparency and allows unlimited corporate funding. They also claim that the anonymity of donors under the scheme can be exploited for unfair advantage. Additionally, it has been noted that a majority of the electoral bonds have gone to the ruling party, the BJP, raising concerns about impartiality.

### [Economics Nobel 2023: How Claudia Goldin shed light on the status of women in the workforce](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/everyday-explainers/economics-nobel-2023-claudia-goldin-explained-8975198/)

Claudia Goldin, a Harvard University professor, has been awarded the Nobel Prize in Economics for her work on women's labor market outcomes. Goldin's research has focused on women's participation in the labor market over the past two centuries and the persistent gender pay gap. She found that older data revealed a history of women's involvement in economic activities related to agriculture and cottage industries before industrialization. The growth of the services sector in the early 20th century, along with factors such as marriage and the contraceptive pill, influenced women's access to higher education and employment opportunities. Goldin highlighted that legislation and societal expectations limited married women's employment prospects, and the introduction of contraceptive pills allowed women to plan their careers and motherhood. Despite women catching up in terms of education and employment fields, the gender pay gap persists. Goldin and her co-authors demonstrated that the pay gap widens when women have children, as they face slower career advancements. This Nobel Prize in Economics is not a part of Alfred Nobel's original will but was established through a donation from Sweden's central bank in 1968. Goldin is the third woman to receive this honor.

In summary, the article explores the recent attack by Hamas in response to attempts at normalizing relations between Israel and Muslim countries in the region. It provides a historical background of the Israel-Palestine conflict, discusses the significance of Jerusalem, and examines the factors contributing to the process of normalization. The article also emphasizes the importance of addressing Palestinian concerns for true normalization to occur.

### [Aditya L1 corrects trajectory as it moves closer to Sun: Why, how was this done?](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/explained-sci-tech/aditya-l1-corrects-trajectory-why-how-was-this-done-8973882/)

Hmm, I am not sure. Email support@chatbase.co for more info.The Indian Space Research Organisation (ISRO) has conducted a Trajectory Correction Maneuver (TCM) for its spacecraft Aditya L1 to ensure it reaches its destination at the Sun Earth Lagrangian point 1. The Aditya L1 is embarking on a 110-day journey through space, one of the longest for an Indian spacecraft since the Mars mission in 2013-2014. Deep space missions require trajectory correction plans to ensure the spacecraft remains on course for its destination. The Aditya L1 spacecraft will be injected into an orbit around L1, and trajectory errors were identified after the Trans Lagrangian Point 1 Insertion maneuver. The spacecraft will orbit around L1 in an irregularly shaped orbit in a plane perpendicular to the line joining the Earth and the Sun. The L1 orbit is challenging to acquire and maintain, and course corrections may be needed during the mission. ISRO has previously performed TCMs during the Mars Orbiter Mission to ensure the spacecraft reached the correct trajectory.

### [What was the Yom Kippur war, and why is the Hamas attack on Israel being compared to it](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/explained-global/what-was-the-yom-kippur-hamas-attack-israel-8973844/)

The recent surprise attack by Hamas on Israel has brought back memories of the Yom Kippur War, which started 50 years ago. This current conflict is being compared to the Yom Kippur War for a couple of reasons. Firstly, it has been the deadliest attack on Israel since the Yom Kippur War in 1973. Hamas gunmen carried out a rampage in Israeli towns, killing at least 400 Israelis and kidnapping civilians. In retaliation, Israeli strikes have killed at least 313 Palestinians. In the Yom Kippur War, over 2,500 Israeli soldiers were killed. 

The second similarity is the criticism within Israel for being unprepared. The attack on Saturday came as a surprise, despite Israel's advanced intelligence and interception systems. This is similar to the Yom Kippur War, where Israel was also caught off guard due to many soldiers being on leave for Yom Kippur, the holiest day in Judaism.

The Yom Kippur War, also known as the October War or the Ramadan War, was fought between Israel, Egypt, and Syria from October 6 to 25, 1973. It was the fourth Arab-Israeli war, following conflicts in 1949, 1956, and 1967. After Israel's decisive victory in the six-day war of 1967, it acquired territories from its neighbors, including the Golan Heights from Syria and the Sinai Peninsula from Egypt. 

Six years later, Egypt and Syria coordinated an attack on Israel. Israel was initially caught off guard and took time to mobilize its soldiers who were on leave for Yom Kippur. While both Syria and Egypt made some gains initially, Israel eventually halted their advances and launched counterstrikes. The intervention of the US and the Soviet Union further escalated tensions between the superpowers. A lasting ceasefire was finally reached on October 25, weakening Israel's previously perceived invincibility. 

Despite Israel's ability to beat back both attackers, the Yom Kippur War is still seen as a game-changer. Israel's scramble to rally troops initially and the heavy casualties it suffered showed that it could be vulnerable in battle. Prime Minister Golda Meir and her cabinet resigned six months after the war. Many analysts believe that Egypt's strategy behind launching the attack was to bruise Israel enough to bring it to the negotiating table. 

Following the war, a peace process was initiated, leading to the 1978 Camp David Accords in which Israel returned the Sinai Peninsula to Egypt. This marked the first instance of an Arab country recognizing Israel as a state. However, for Syria, the war had no positive outcomes as Israel continued to occupy the strategically important Golan Heights.

### [Why the RBI’s Open Market Operation plan caught the market by surprise](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/explained-economics/rbi-open-market-operation-8972699/)

Hmm, I am not sure. Email support@chatbase.co for more info.The Reserve Bank of India's (RBI) recent announcement to consider open market operation (OMO) sales of government securities took the bond market by surprise. The central bank did not specify a timeline for this proposal, causing the yield on benchmark 10-year government bonds to rise by 12 basis points to 7.34%. This unexpected move led to speculation of an OMO being implemented soon, which would tighten liquidity in the system. The surprise element stemmed from the absence of a specific calendar for these operations, leaving market participants on edge as OMOs could potentially be announced at any time.

The market was not anticipating such a measure by the RBI to withdraw excess liquidity, adding a hawkish tone to the monetary policy. Liquidity is expected to tighten further due to cash withdrawals during the upcoming festival season. RBI Governor Shaktikanta Das mentioned that OMO sales may be considered to manage liquidity, but the timing and quantity of such operations will depend on evolving liquidity conditions.

OMOs are used by the RBI to adjust rupee liquidity conditions in the market on a lasting basis. If there is excess liquidity, the central bank sells government securities to remove rupee liquidity. Conversely, when liquidity conditions are tight, the RBI purchases securities to release liquidity into the market. OMOs are employed as a tool to control inflation and money supply. However, when liquidity is sucked out, it can lead to higher bond yields, as the RBI introduces more government securities into the market, causing bond buyers to demand higher interest rates on these securities.

The RBI's emphasis on active liquidity management during the post-policy press conference indicates its inclination towards tighter liquidity conditions in the future. This approach is driven by concerns about inflation risks and financial stability, aligning with the central bank's objective of keeping inflation at 4%. The RBI aims to achieve this target through liquidity management, particularly during the October-May period when cash withdrawals are high due to festive and wedding seasons.

The current liquidity position is such that the RBI may opt for OMO sales auctions of government securities to absorb any excess liquidity. In September, the RBI conducted OMO sales in the secondary market, resulting in net sales amounting to Rs 6,200 crore. Despite the gradual withdrawal of the incremental cash reserve ratio (i-CRR) implemented in the August Monetary Policy Committee meeting, systemic liquidity remained in deficit due to tax outflows and GST payments. The government's cash balance with the RBI is estimated at around Rs 2.7 lakh crore, and an increase in government spending and the withdrawal of i-CRR could boost systemic liquidity. However, the festive season may also lead to increased currency demand, offsetting any liquidity increase.

### [How China invaded Tibet and annexed it](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/explained-history/china-tibet-invasion-annexation-dalai-lama-8972679/)

This article discusses the Chinese invasion of Tibet in 1950 and its long-lasting impact. It emphasizes how the invasion was initially unknown to most Tibetans due to the remote location of the region and the slow spread of news. The Chinese Communist Party had both ideological and pragmatic motivations for annexing Tibet, wanting to secure the border and access its natural resources while also seeing it as a feudal-theocracy in need of liberation. However, Tibet had historically existed independently, with its own culture, language, and religion. Tense negotiations took place between Lhasa and Beijing, but China steadily built up forces at Tibet's border to exert pressure. The People's Liberation Army invaded Tibet in October 1950, capturing the town of Chamdo and forcing the Tibetan garrison to surrender. China then sent the defeated governor of Chamdo to Lhasa to propose the seventeen point agreement to the Dalai Lama. Under duress, Tibetan plenipotentiaries signed the agreement in May 1951, subordinating Tibet to China while promising non-interference in domestic affairs, culture, and religion. However, China soon violated the agreement and introduced communist reforms in Tibet, eroding its autonomy and imposing cultural and economic changes. Massive resistance grew in Tibet by 1954, and in 1959, a revolt erupted in Lhasa amid fears that the CCP might arrest the Dalai Lama. Chinese reprisals were brutal, resulting in the deaths of thousands and the destruction of monasteries. The Dalai Lama fled to India, repudiated the seventeen point agreement, and declared himself as Tibet's legitimate representative. Since then, China has maintained absolute control over Tibet, suppressing dissent, placing its own people in religious positions, and encouraging Han Chinese immigration, which has affected Tibet's demographics and culture.

### [Why India is launching a national framework for climate services](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/explained-climate/national-framework-for-climate-services-nfcs-8972568/)

Hmm, I am not sure. Email support@chatbase.co for more info.India is launching its first national-level framework for climate services, called the National Framework for Climate Services (NFCS). Spearheaded by the India Meteorological Department (IMD), the NFCS aims to provide climate information and services to key sectors such as agriculture, energy, disaster management, health, and water. The NFCS is based on the Global Framework for Climate Services (GFCS), which is a partnership of governments and organizations at a global level. The GFCS aims to produce and improve the usage of climate information and services. The NFCS will focus on generating high-quality data on weather parameters and creating long-term projections and scenarios. The five major components of the GFCS are Observations and Monitoring, Research, Modelling and Prediction, Climate Services Information System, User Interface Platform, and Capacity Building. The IMD will be the nodal agency for implementing the national framework in India. Initially, the NFCS will work on bridging gaps between various agencies that require climate services. The IMD plans to strengthen its observational network and improve data inflow to run weather and climate models for deriving climate predictions. The framework aims to support efforts in preparing for new climate conditions and adapting to their impacts on various sectors. Several countries have already launched their own NFCS, and India will soon release its statement after receiving consensus from key stakeholders.

### [Russia may pull out of CTBT: How the treaty tried — and failed — to stop nuclear testing](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/explained-global/nuclear-test-ban-treaty-ctbt-8971599/)

Hmm, I am not sure. Email support@chatbase.co for more info.Russian President Vladimir Putin announced that Russia may revoke its ratification of the Comprehensive Nuclear Test Ban Treaty (CTBT) to be on equal terms with the United States, not to resume nuclear testing. The CTBT is a multilateral treaty that prohibits all nuclear explosions. While Russia ratified the agreement in 2000, the US has yet to do so. The CTBT was adopted by the United Nations General Assembly in 1996 but is still in the ratification stage, with 18 countries yet to ratify it. The treaty came into being due to concerns about the health and environmental effects of nuclear tests. Previous attempts to curb nuclear testing included the Limited Nuclear Test-Ban Treaty (LTBT) in 1963 and the Threshold Test Ban Treaty (TTBT) in 1974. The breakthrough for a comprehensive test ban came after the end of the Cold War, leading to the adoption of the CTBT in 1996. Since then, there have been 10 nuclear tests conducted by various countries, including India, Pakistan, and North Korea. To enter into force, the CTBT requires ratification by 44 specific nuclear technology holder countries, eight of which have yet to ratify it: China, Egypt, India, Iran, Israel, North Korea, Pakistan, and the United States. UN Secretary-General António Guterres has urged these countries to ratify the treaty.

### [When can a bill be designated as a ‘money bill’: SC to hear challenge](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/explained-law/sc-challenge-centre-money-bill-key-legislation-8970978/)

Hmm, I am not sure. Email support@chatbase.co for more info.Hmm, I am not sure. Email support@chatbase.co for more info.Chief Justice of India, DY Chandrachud, announced on Friday that a seven-judge bench will be established to hear a group of pleas challenging the government's use of the money bill route to pass certain legislations. The Chief Justice made this observation while hearing a plea specifically challenging amendments to the Prevention of Money Laundering Act (PMLA). The seven-judge bench will examine whether the legislation in question was validly categorized as a money bill or if it was done to avoid scrutiny from the Rajya Sabha. A money bill is defined by Article 110 of the Constitution as a bill that deals with taxation or the appropriation of public funds. It can only be introduced in the Lok Sabha, and if the Speaker categorizes a bill as a money bill, it does not need to be passed by the Rajya Sabha. The Modi government has introduced several key legislations as money bills, bypassing the Rajya Sabha. The constitutionality of three key legislations, including the PMLA, the Aadhaar Act, and amendments altering service conditions of tribunals, will be examined by the larger bench to determine if they qualified as money bills. The Supreme Court had previously upheld the PMLA and the Aadhaar Act but struck down the tribunal amendments as unconstitutional. The court will reconsider the validity of these laws in light of whether they were rightly categorized as money bills.

### [Nobel Prize in Literature: In Norwegian laureate Jon Fosse’s ‘slow prose’, shades of Ibsen and Derrida](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/explained-culture/in-norwegian-laureate-jon-fosses-slow-prose-shades-of-ibsen-and-derrida-8970388/)

Norwegian playwright, poet, novelist, essayist, and translator Jon Fosse has won the Nobel Prize in Literature for his focus on the subterranean that connects personal experiences with universal themes. Fosse's work, which includes innovative plays, novels, poetry collections, essays, children's books, and translations, gives voice to the unsayable. While he is known for his plays, Fosse has also gained recognition for his prose. In his acceptance statement, Fosse expressed gratitude and a sense of overwhelm, seeing the award as a recognition of literature that aims to be literature above all else. Fosse's literary career began with the novel "Red Black" in 1983, and he has since written several notable plays and novels. His work is often compared to that of Henrik Ibsen and Jacques Derrida for its modernist concerns and ability to tap into the unspoken.

### [Nobel Prize for Literature 2023: Meet the winner, the Norwegian author Jon Fosse](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/everyday-explainers/nobelliterature-who-is-jon-fosse-8970192/)

Norwegian author Jon Olav Fosse has been awarded the Nobel Prize for Literature 2023 for his innovative plays and prose that give voice to the unsayable. Fosse's work presents everyday situations that are relatable and expresses powerful human emotions of anxiety and powerlessness in simple terms. He is regarded as a major innovator in contemporary theatre for his ability to evoke man's loss of orientation and provide access to a deeper experience close to divinity. Fosse writes in Norwegian Nynorsk, the least common version of Norwegian, and sees the award as recognition for this language and the movement promoting it. He started writing novels before switching to plays and has become one of Norway's most-performed dramatists. His works have been translated into over 40 languages, and he has written around 40 plays along with novels, short stories, children's books, poetry, and essays. Fosse's writing style is characterized by simple, minimal, searing dialogue similar to Samuel Beckett and Harold Pinter. His themes explore the absurdity, futility, and power of the human condition, everyday confusions and irresolutions, and the difficulty of forming connections through conversation. While Fosse has been celebrated in Europe for a long time, he is not as popular in English-speaking countries like the UK and US due to difficulties in relating to his themes and connecting with his plays. However, his popularity has been increasing in these geographies in recent years.

The Punjab Chief Minister, Bhagwant Singh Mann, has announced that the state will ban the cultivation of the pusa-44 paddy variety from next year. This variety was widely grown in Punjab and covered 70 to 80 percent of the state's total area under paddy cultivation. Farmers were satisfied with its high yield, but the agriculture department and Punjab Agricultural University discouraged its cultivation due to declining groundwater levels. The government aims to conserve one month of irrigation water by banning the variety, as well as address issues such as stubble burning and air pollution. Pusa-44 has an extended maturity period, leading to a narrow window for stubble disposal between paddy harvesting and wheat sowing. The variety is grown extensively in several districts of Punjab, where stubble-burning incidents are high. The ban is expected to have an impact on farmers' income, as pusa-44 yields more than other varieties.

The court granted this injunction to protect the revenues of Star India, the official broadcaster of the World Cup, as rogue websites that engage in piracy are likely to illegally broadcast the matches. Dynamic injunctions are granted before copyrighted works are publicly released or created to prevent irreparable loss due to online piracy. The Delhi High Court has previously passed similar injunctions to protect copyrighted works, including films and series. Star India cited previous cases and its exclusive rights acquired from the ICC to seek the injunction. The court's ruling falls under Section 37 of the 1957 Copyright Act, which grants broadcasting organizations special rights and specifies actions that constitute infringement. Exceptions to copyright infringement are included under Section 39 if the reproduction can be considered fair dealing.

### [Glacial lake outburst flood kills 14 in Sikkim, 102 people missing: What is GLOF, and why does it happen?](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/explained-climate/glacial-lake-outburst-flood-glof-sikkim-8968562/)

Fourteen people have been killed and 102 are missing in Sikkim, India, after the South Lhonak Lake burst due to heavy rains. The release of water caused flooding in downstream areas, including four districts. Glacial lakes like South Lhonak Lake are dangerous because they are mostly dammed by unstable ice or sediment, and if the boundary breaks, it can cause flooding. Glacial lake outburst floods (GLOF) can be triggered by earthquakes, heavy rains, or ice avalanches. Rising global temperatures have led to the rapid melting of glaciers in Sikkim Himalayan, resulting in the expansion of glacial lakes. There are currently over 300 glacial lakes in Sikkim Himalayan, with 10 identified as vulnerable to outburst floods. South Lhonak Lake has been under observation for years due to its significant increase in size. In 2016, authorities syphoned off water from the lake to mitigate the risk of a GLOF.

### [Nobel Prize in Chemistry for 2023: What exactly have the three scientists been honoured for?](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/everyday-explainers/nobel-prize-in-chemistry-for-2023-what-have-the-three-done-8968403/)

The Nobel Prize in Chemistry for 2023 has been awarded to Moungi Bawendi, Louis Brus, and Alexei Ekimov for their discovery and synthesis of quantum dots. Quantum dots are small particles of nanotechnology that have various applications, including in televisions, LED lamps, and guiding surgeons during tumor removal. While scientists had theorized about the existence of these particles, the three laureates were able to create high-quality quantum dots that could be practically used. Ekimov was the first to make notable discoveries but his research was not accessible beyond the Iron Curtain. Ekimov and Brus independently observed how the size of particles affected their absorption of light, leading to the realization that particle size alters characteristics and behavior. Bawendi further improved the creation of quantum dots using different methods. Quantum dots are now utilized in computer and television screens, LED lamps, biochemistry, and medicine.

French physicist Pierre Agostini, Hungarian physicist Ferenc Krausz, and French physicist Anne L'Huillier have been awarded the 2023 Nobel Prize in Physics for their work on generating attosecond pulses of light to study electron dynamics in matter. Their breakthrough allows scientists to observe processes at the atomic and subatomic levels that were previously impossible to observe. ### [What UAPA sections have been invoked against NewsClick](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/explained-law/what-uapa-sections-have-been-invoked-against-newsclick-8967236/)

Hmm, I am not sure. Email support@chatbase.co for more info.The Delhi Police has sealed the office of news portal NewsClick, accusing it of receiving money for pro-China propaganda. The police have filed an FIR against the portal under the Unlawful Activities (Prevention) Act (UAPA), a stringent anti-terror law. The main allegation is that NewsClick received illegal funding from China through the United States. The FIR includes various sections of the UAPA, including Section 16, which deals with punishment for terrorist acts. This section defines "terrorist act" as any act that threatens India's unity, integrity, security, or sovereignty or strikes terror in the people. Other provisions invoked against NewsClick include unlawful activities, raising funds for terrorist acts, conspiracy, and offences by companies and trusts. The UAPA gives the state more powers compared to the Indian Penal Code (IPC) by relaxing timelines for filing chargesheets and imposing stringent conditions for bail. The Supreme Court has defined prima facie narrowly in relation to denying bail under the UAPA, stating that courts must only consider whether a prima facie case can be made out and not analyze evidence or circumstances. Section 43D(5) of the UAPA states that a person accused of an offence punishable under Chapters IV and VI of the Act cannot be released on bail unless the Public Prosecutor has been given an opportunity to be heard and there are reasonable grounds to believe that the accusation is prima facie true.

### [Why China used a torn painting as a symbol of ‘hope for reunification’ with Taiwan](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/explained-global/why-china-used-a-torn-painting-as-a-symbol-of-hope-for-reunification-with-taiwan-8967024/)

On China's National Day, the Chinese military released an animated short film that depicted two pieces of a torn historical scroll painting, one kept in China and the other in Taiwan, joining together. The film symbolized the shared cultural heritage and hope for reunification between the two sides of the Taiwan Straits. China claims Taiwan as its territory and has increased its military presence around the island in recent years. President Xi Jinping has even stated that Beijing is ready to take Taiwan by force if necessary. The painting in question is called "Dwelling in the Fuchun Mountains," created by artist Huang Gongwang in the 14th century. It is considered a great classical work and was passed down to art collectors over time. Attempts were made to restore it, but it became separated into two parts. One part is now in the collection of the Zhejiang Provincial Museum in China, while the other is in Taipei, Taiwan. The animated film released by the Chinese military features two elves representing the two pieces of the painting, magically making it whole again at the end of the movie. China claims that Taiwan was part of its territory until Japan occupied it after a war in 1895. After World War II, a civil war between the Communists and Nationalists led to both sides claiming to be the "official" China. However, as China's economy grew and opened up to the world, more countries recognized it as China officially, maintaining only informal relations with Taiwan. Currently, only 13 countries have diplomatic relations with Taiwan. The Democratic Progressive Party (DPP) winning elections in 2004 brought a change as they have been wary of China's control over Taiwan. President Xi Jinping's increasing power since 2013 has also led to heightened rhetoric on Taiwan from China. The Chinese military's release of a film showing the reunification of the painting is seen as part of its larger aims related to Taiwan. The film also references the separation of cultural relics during the relocation of the Palace Museum to Taiwan over half a century ago. The painting has been recreated for the ongoing 2023 Asian Games in China's Hangzhou.

L'Huillier, Agostini, and Krausz's work has brought humanity closer to studying the movement of electrons and understanding mechanisms governed by them. ### [Why free speech is not a priority in Southeast Asia](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/explained-global/free-speech-not-priority-in-southeast-asia-8965588/)

Southeast Asian countries have some of the worst press freedom and media rights in the world, according to a report by the Pew Research Center. Governments in the region, ranging from communist states to tentative democracies, tend to limit free speech in order to protect national "harmony." This is reflected in global press freedom rankings, with Vietnam ranked third worst in the world. The deterioration of free speech internationally is attributed to increased aggression from authorities and growing animosity towards journalists on social media. A majority of respondents in three out of four Southeast Asian states agree that national harmony should come before free speech, although younger and more educated individuals are more likely to support the ability to criticize governments and prioritize free speech. Differences based on religion were also found, with Thai Muslims more likely than Thai Buddhists to prioritize harmony over free speech. Critics argue that Southeast Asia's strict laws on hate speech and other legislation do not actually defend social harmony in practice.

### [Nobel Prize in Medicine 2023: How Katalin Kariko and Drew Weissman’s work helped develop Covid vaccines](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/everyday-explainers/nobel-prize-in-medicine-2023-work-helped-develop-covid-vaccines-8965450/)

The 2023 Nobel Prize in Physiology or Medicine has been awarded to scientists Katalin Kariko and Drew Weissman for their work on mRNA vaccines against Covid-19. Their groundbreaking findings have revolutionized our understanding of how mRNA interacts with the immune system, leading to the rapid development of vaccines during the pandemic. Traditional vaccines rely on introducing weakened or dead viruses into the body, but mRNA technology uses messenger RNA to deliver instructions to cells to produce the necessary proteins to fight a specific virus. Kariko and Weissman discovered that lab-grown mRNA was recognized as foreign by dendritic cells, triggering an inflammatory response. By modifying the bases in the mRNA, they were able to significantly reduce this response. Their work laid the foundation for the development of mRNA vaccines by companies like Moderna and Pfizer. Kariko is a Hungarian scientist who has worked at various institutions, including BioNTech RNA Pharmaceuticals, while Weissman is an American researcher based at the University of Pennsylvania.

Mon, 02 Oct 2023 12:00:43 +0000
### [Bihar caste survey data out: Why it was legally challenged — and why the court found it ‘perfectly valid’](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/explained-law/bihar-caste-survey-legal-challenged-8965424/)

The Bihar caste survey conducted by the state government has revealed that Other Backward Classes (OBCs) and Extremely Backward Classes (EBCs) make up more than 63% of the population of Bihar. This is in contrast to the 27% reservation that the OBC population receives in government jobs and educational institutions. Bihar Chief Minister Nitish Kumar is expected to use this data for political gain both at the state and national level. 

The caste survey faced challenges during its second phase, with the Patna High Court initially staying the survey in May. However, the court later deemed the survey to be valid. The grounds for the challenge included claims that the survey violated citizens' right to privacy and that the state did not have the power to conduct such a survey. The court ruled that permissible restrictions can be imposed on the right to privacy in the state's legitimate interests, and it accepted the Bihar government's assurance of data security. The court clarified that the survey aimed to identify backward classes for development schemes, not to discriminate against individuals or groups. 

The competence of the state government to conduct the survey was also questioned, with petitioners arguing that only the Union government has the power to conduct a census. The Bihar government argued that a caste census was previously conducted by the Union government in 2011, and that it had the power to collect statistics under the concurrent list. The court ruled that the state's actions were valid and within its competence, as it aimed to provide development with justice and did not violate privacy rights.

Overall, the Patna High Court upheld the validity of the Bihar caste survey, stating that it serves a compelling public interest and is in line with the state's legitimate interests.

### [An Expert Explains: In alarm over Conocarpus trees, echo of faulty policies on invasive exotics](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/explained-climate/expert-explains-gujarat-conocarpus-trees-invasive-plants-8964032/)

Concerns have been raised over the management of the invasive Conocarpus species of trees in India, leading to bans in Gujarat and Telangana. This has drawn attention to the selection of species in urban landscaping projects, which often favor exotic species for beautification. Many previously introduced species for greening or ornamental purposes have become invasive, driving native species to extinction and incurring significant costs for their management. The two species of Conocarpus trees, which are easily propagated in nurseries, have caused pollen allergies and respiratory problems. The proliferation of non-native species is facilitated by their lack of pests or pathogens in new habitats and minimal aftercare requirements. It is recommended that landscape managers choose native species for long-term sustainability and refer to existing information on these species before large-scale use. Monitoring the import and use of horticultural plants is necessary to protect native species, ecosystems, and public health. Clear guidelines are needed for landscape planners and land managers in mega greening projects, as urban greens contribute significantly to mitigating greenhouse gas emissions. India's climate commitments require a focus on nature-based solutions, such as sustainable biodiversity and ecosystem services in urban areas.

### [The story of Mahatma Gandhi’s portrait on Indian banknotes](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/story-mahatma-gandhi-portrait-indian-banknotes-8963152/)

This article discusses the portrait of Mahatma Gandhi on Indian official currency notes. While many prominent photographers captured images of Gandhi throughout his life, the most widely circulated image of him is the one on the currency notes. The portrait on the notes is a cut-out of a photograph taken in 1946 where Gandhi is seen standing with British politician Lord Frederick William Pethick-Lawrence. The identity of the photographer and the person who selected the photo remain unknown.

The article explains that it wasn't until 1996, many decades after India gained independence in 1947, that Gandhi became a permanent feature on all legal banknotes issued by the Reserve Bank of India (RBI). The RBI's Department of Currency Management designs the rupee notes and needs approval from the central bank and the union government.

The article mentions that Gandhi first appeared on Indian currency in 1969, with a special series issued to commemorate his 100th birth anniversary. In October 1987, a series of 500-rupee currency notes featuring Gandhi was launched. Before independent India, the RBI continued to issue notes from the colonial period featuring King George VI. The design of the 1-rupee note was changed in 1949 to replace King George VI with the lion capital of Ashoka pillar at Sarnath.

Over the years, new designs and motifs were introduced on the banknotes to represent different aspects of India. In the 1990s, the RBI introduced the 'Mahatma Gandhi series' to replace the former Ashoka pillar banknotes. Various security features were also added to combat counterfeiting. In 2016, the 'Mahatma Gandhi new series' of banknotes was announced, with additional security features and the inclusion of the Swachh Bharat Abhiyan logo on the back of the notes.

The article concludes by mentioning suggestions made to include other personalities on currency notes, such as Lord Ganesha and Goddess Lakshmi. However, the RBI committee decided that Mahatma Gandhi best represents the ethos of India and rejected the suggestions to replace his portrait with any other leader.

Overall, the article provides insight into the history and significance of Mahatma Gandhi's portrait on Indian currency notes and discusses the various changes made to the designs over time.

Sat, 30 Sep 2023 13:31:14 +0000
### [Why Vivek Ramaswamy wants to end birthright citizenship in the US](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/explained-global/vivek-ramaswamy-birthright-citizenship-us-8963079/)

Indian-American presidential candidate Vivek Ramaswamy has stated that he would end birthright citizenship in the US if elected, echoing a proposal made by former President Donald Trump in 2015. Ramaswamy argues that children of undocumented immigrants should not be granted citizenship as their parents broke the law to be in the country. The debate over birthright citizenship centers around the 14th Amendment to the US Constitution, which guarantees citizenship to individuals born in the US. The amendment was introduced in 1858 to overturn a Supreme Court ruling that denied African Americans citizenship rights. It wasn't until 1898, in the case of 'United States v. Wong Kim Ark,' that the Supreme Court confirmed birthright citizenship for most people born in the US. Proponents of ending birthright citizenship argue that it should not apply to children of illegal immigrants and question whether they are subject to US jurisdiction. Supporters of birthright citizenship believe that any person in the US is subject to US laws regardless of immigration status and argue against racial undertones in the debate. Birthright citizenship remains a recurring topic in American politics, with Trump previously proposing to abolish it but facing constitutional barriers.

### [Four reasons why bank credit growth is expected to moderate in FY24](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/explained-economics/bank-credit-growth-moderate-fy24-8962582/)

Hmm, I am not sure. Email support@chatbase.co for more info.According to domestic rating agency Crisil Ratings, the growth of credit in the banking sector is expected to be lower at 13-13.5% in fiscal year 2023-24. This projection comes after a strong credit growth of 15.9% in fiscal year 2022-23, driven by a broad-based economic recovery, healthier balance sheets, and a lower base from the previous two fiscal years.

However, this estimate excludes the impact of the merger between HDFC Ltd and HDFC Bank in fiscal year 2024. Care Ratings also projected a growth rate of 13-13.5% for bank credit in fiscal year 2024, excluding the merger's impact.

As of September 8, 2023, bank credit grew by 19.81% on a year-on-year basis to Rs 150.38 lakh crore, including the impact of the merger. Excluding the merger's impact, bank credit grew by 15.08%. Year-to-date, bank credit (including the merger) has risen by 8.6% as of September 8.

In the first quarter of the current fiscal year, personal loans (housing and non-housing) recorded some deceleration but continued to grow above the overall credit growth. Non-food credit grew by 15% in August, while personal loans growth decelerated to 18.3% due to moderation in credit to housing.

Credit growth to the industry registered a growth of 6.1%, while credit to the services sector accelerated to 20.7% primarily due to non-banking financial companies (NBFCs) and commercial real estate.

Crisil attributes the moderation in bank credit growth to four factors. First, GDP growth is expected to decline to 6% in fiscal year 2023-24, impacting overall credit growth. Second, inflation is projected to ease due to the softening of commodity prices, reducing the demand for working capital loans. Third, the substitution of debt capital markets has decreased, impacting wholesale credit growth. Finally, the high-base effect from strong credit growth in fiscal year 2023 will contribute to lower bank credit growth this fiscal year.

Crisil expects bank credit growth to improve to 13.5-14% in fiscal year 2024-25 as economic growth picks up. Retail credit is expected to remain a key growth driver, expanding steadily at 19-20% each fiscal year.

The revival of private industrial capex is slower than expected, but capex announcements and higher capacity utilization levels suggest a ground-level pickup in capex next fiscal. On the services side, demand from non-banks is expected to support corporate credit growth. In the MSME segment, credit demand is expected to be steady due to the government's initiatives and the productivity-linked incentive scheme. Home loan demand is expected to be steady, and unsecured loans are expected to grow faster due to digitalization and borrowing for discretionary spending.

### [OSIRIS-REx and Bennu: Sample return missions explained](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/explained-sci-tech/osiris-rex-nasa-return-missions-explained-8960460/)

NASA's OSIRIS-REx spacecraft has spent seven years collecting and returning samples from the asteroid Bennu. After mapping the asteroid and collecting about 250 grams of rock, the spacecraft is now returning to Earth. The samples will be released over the Great Salt Lake Desert in Utah and then transferred to NASA's Johnson Space Center in Houston. OSIRIS-REx will then continue its journey to the asteroid Apophis. Sample return missions provide valuable information about our solar system and early Earth history. The samples from Bennu are expected to give insight into the formation of the Sun and planets. NASA will keep 25% of the samples and distribute the rest among mission members and international partners. Sample return missions have been conducted in the past, including the Apollo missions to the Moon and Japan's Hayabusa missions to asteroids. Future missions, such as Japan's MMX and the European Space Agency and NASA's Mars sample return mission, aim to bring back samples from Mars and its moons.

### [Jewar Airport gets ‘DXN’ code: How the IATA assigns unique codes to each airport](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/everyday-explainers/jewar-airport-dxn-code-iata-codes-8959951/)

Hmm, I am not sure. Email support@chatbase.co for more info.The upcoming Noida International Airport (NIA) in Jewar has been awarded its own unique international three-letter code, 'DXN', by the International Air Transport Association (IATA). The CEO of NIA, Christoph Schnellmann, explained that the code is significant as it represents the connectivity of Delhi-Noida with the world through the airport. The "D" in DXN signifies Delhi, the national capital, while the "N" represents Noida, indicating the presence of the airport in the western UP area. The letter "X" signifies connectivity within India and the world. Phase 1 of the airport is expected to be operational by the end of 2024, with a capacity of 12 million passengers per year and a 3.9-km-long north runway. Each airport has two unique codes, one assigned by IATA and the other by the International Civil Aviation Organization (ICAO). The IATA codes are used for passenger-facing operations, while the ICAO codes are used by industry professionals. Kiran Jain, COO of NIA, stated that the airport code is a major milestone and will not change as long as the airport exists. The assignment of airport codes is governed by IATA resolution 763 and published twice a year in the IATA airline coding directory.

### [How MS Swaminathan triggered the Green Revolution and helped India achieve food security](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/ms-swaminathan-father-green-revolution-explained-8959892/)

Hmm, I am not sure. Email support@chatbase.co for more info.Monkomb Sambasivan Swaminathan, also known as the 'Father of the Green Revolution', passed away at the age of 98. He played a significant role in transforming farming in the 1960s and '70s, leading to India achieving food security. Swaminathan was highly dedicated to agriculture and conducted extensive research in the field throughout his career.

He held various positions in agricultural institutions both in India and abroad. Notable roles included serving as the Independent Chairman of the Food and Agricultural Organisation Council, President of the International Union for the Conservation of Nature and Natural Resources, President of the World Wide Fund for Nature (India), and the Director General of the Indian Council of Agricultural Research.

Swaminathan's interest in agriculture was sparked during the Bengal famine in 1942-43. This event, combined with Mahatma Gandhi's call for the Quit India Movement, led him to study agriculture instead of pursuing a medical career. Fascinated by the science of genetics, he decided to focus his research on genetics and breeding, recognizing the impact that improved crop strains could have on farmers.

His research on rice led him to work at the Central Rice Research Institute, where he pioneered the transfer of genes for fertilizer response from Japonica varieties to Indica varieties. This breakthrough laid the foundation for developing high-yielding crop varieties that could thrive in good soil fertility and water management.

Swaminathan's most significant achievement was his contribution to the Green Revolution, which involved providing farmers in Punjab, Haryana, and western Uttar Pradesh with high-yielding variety seeds, irrigation facilities, and fertilizers. As a result, wheat production in India increased dramatically, transforming the nation from a food-deficient country to self-sufficiency. This achievement instilled confidence in Indian farmers and disproved the notion that India was reliant on wheat imports from the US.

He collaborated with American scientist Norman Borlaug, who incorporated dwarfing genes into spring wheat varieties suitable for the Indian climate. Their joint efforts led to the development of Gaines, a high-yielding dwarf wheat variety. This breakthrough marked the beginning of the "Wheat Revolution" in India.

Despite the success of the Green Revolution, Swaminathan acknowledged its shortcomings, such as favoring already prosperous farmers and unsustainable farming practices. He advocated for the conservation of soil fertility, responsible use of pesticides and herbicides, and scientific management of underground water resources.

In recognition of his contributions, Swaminathan received numerous accolades, including the first World Food Prize Laureate in 1987. His work saved millions from food deprivation and secured India's food self-sufficiency.

Swaminathan's legacy extends beyond his scientific achievements. As the head of the National Commission on Farmers, he advocated for fair prices for farmers, recommending that the Minimum Support Price should be at least 50 percent higher than the weighted average cost of production.

Monkomb Sambasivan Swaminathan's pioneering work in agriculture has left an indelible mark on India's farming landscape, ensuring food security and improving the lives of millions of farmers.

### [Nearly 69 lakh cancer deaths among Indian women were preventable: What a study says on gender and the disease](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/explained-health/cancers-gender-problem-8959239/)

Hmm, I am not sure. Email support@chatbase.co for more info.A new report by the Lancet Commission on gender inequity in cancer care reveals that around 63% of premature deaths due to cancers in Indian women could have been prevented through risk reduction, screening, and diagnosis. Additionally, 37% of these deaths could have been averted with timely and optimal treatment. The report, titled 'Women, Power and Cancer', highlights that although men are at a higher risk of cancers that affect both genders, cancer incidence and mortality in women remain high. Globally, women account for 48% of new cancer cases and 44% of cancer deaths. The report emphasizes that women face challenges in accessing timely and appropriate care due to a lack of knowledge, decision-making power, financial resources, and availability of services at the primary level. Women are also more likely than men to lack the knowledge and power to make informed decisions about their healthcare. Furthermore, they are more likely to experience financial hardship due to cancer. The report also notes that women are underrepresented as leaders in providing cancer care and often face gender-based discrimination and sexual harassment. They also constitute the largest unpaid workforce in this field. The report estimates that the value of unpaid cancer caregiving by women is about 3.66% of India's national health expenditure. Dr Abhishek Shankar from the All India Institute of Medical Sciences explains that there is a gendered aspect to cancer care, with healthcare-seeking behavior being lower among women, especially those from poor sections of society. He adds that treatment for women is often not prioritized, leading to worse outcomes compared to men. The report calls for societal changes alongside increased knowledge about cancer prevention. It suggests creating awareness campaigns to encourage people, especially women, to come forward for screening and seek care. The report also recommends developing laws and policies that reduce exposure to known cancer risks and ensuring equitable access to research resources, leadership positions, and funding opportunities for women in the field of cancer care.

### [Bonding with the world: Will more inflows give India’s economy the booster dose?](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/explained-economics/jp-morgan-indian-government-bonds-8959037/)

JP Morgan Chase & Co has recently decided to include Indian government bonds in its emerging markets index, which could lead to a surge in dollar inflows into India. Other global investment majors are also considering including India in their investment index baskets. While there are differing views on when the foreign funds may start flowing into the country, it is widely agreed that this surge in flows will positively impact India's fiscal and balance of payments dynamics. The inclusion of government bonds in JP Morgan's index will be staggered over a 10-month period, starting in June 2024. Analysts estimate that this inclusion could result in inflows of around $25-30 billion into the government securities market. Other major index providers, such as FTSE Russell, are also reportedly considering including Indian bonds in their indices. The impact of these inflows is expected to include a downward pressure on interest rates, higher profits for the corporate sector, a potential boost to the bond market, a stronger rupee, and a rise in foreign exchange reserves. However, there are concerns about the macro-prudential risks associated with heavy foreign debt flows, as well as the potential for capital flight and market volatility. The RBI will play a crucial role in monitoring and intervening to maintain stability in the financial markets.

### [Why Odisha wants to have its own tiger census, how it will go about it](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/odisha-tiger-census-explained-8958968/)

The Odisha government has announced its plans to conduct a tiger census after questioning the findings of the All-India Tiger Estimation (AITE) 2022. The AITE report stated that more than half of the tigers in Odisha have disappeared since 2016, with one tiger reserve left with no tigers. Disagreeing with the AITE's methodology, Odisha will use the camera trap method along with other approaches for its own census. The state aims to have a more accurate estimation of the tiger population and their habitats for better monitoring and protection. Odisha intends to conduct the counting more frequently than the AITE, which is done every four years. The survey will be carried out in the rest of Odisha, excluding coastal districts, and will include camera-trapping and DNA analysis. The survey will be conducted in phases, starting with a preparatory phase and ending with the analysis of camera trap images and DNA findings before compiling the results. The success of the census will depend on the sincerity of forest officers, and the state's survey aims to take place annually instead of every four years.

The Center for Countering Digital Hate (CCDH) found that x failed to take action against 99% of hate posts by Twitter blue subscribers. Musk's changes within the company, including laying off employees and restoring barred accounts, have been attributed to the deterioration of safety standards on the platform. The dilution of the verification process has also led to the creation of fake accounts. X has sued the CCDH in response to their findings, claiming harm to its business.

### [Why the delimitation exercise is seen as a political battle between the BJP and the rest](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/explained-politics/womens-reservation-bill-political-issue-of-delimitation-8957721/)

Hmm, I am not sure. Email support@chatbase.co for more info.The Women's Reservation Bill, which aims to provide a 33% quota for women in the Lok Sabha and state Assemblies, was recently passed by both Houses of Parliament. However, its fate is uncertain due to its connection to the delimitation exercise. Opposition parties have criticized the government for linking the implementation of women's reservation with delimitation, arguing that there is no reason for this association. The government's decision to make women's reservation contingent on delimitation seems to serve multiple objectives. First, the delimitation exercise will increase the number of Lok Sabha and Assembly constituencies, potentially maintaining the current number of male legislators while accommodating women's reservation. Second, it appears to be an attempt to exert pressure on opposition parties, particularly those from south India, to support the delimitation exercise. Delimitation is a constitutional mandate that adjusts the number of seats and their boundaries based on population data after each Census. However, the number of seats in the Lok Sabha and state Assemblies has remained unchanged for the past 50 years due to opposition from parties in South India. These parties are reluctant to allow delimitation as it would result in a significant increase in Lok Sabha seats in North Indian states, benefiting the BJP the most. The fear of male politicians losing their seats has been a major obstacle in the implementation of women's reservation. By connecting women's reservation to delimitation, the government aims to ensure the preservation of the current male politicians' political fortunes. If the delimitation exercise results in an increase in the number of Lok Sabha seats to 770, 257 seats would be reserved for women, leaving 513 seats for men to contest. This would simplify the accommodation of male leaders within political parties. The opposition parties have recognized the government's larger agenda, which is to make the delimitation exercise inevitable and to use any resistance to it as a means to accuse the opposition of hindering the women's reservation bill. However, the opposition is not willing to surrender and has raised concerns about the potential reduction in the representation of South Indian states if the implementation of women's reservation is linked to delimitation. Former law minister Kapil Sibal has warned that the linkage with delimitation could further delay the implementation of women's reservation beyond 2029. Delimitation aims to ensure equitable representation in the Lok Sabha and state Assemblies based on population, but there is no defined number of seats. If the number of seats were to remain the same as in 1977, the Lok Sabha would need to expand to nearly 1,400 seats due to population growth. However, the maximum capacity of the new Lok Sabha is set at 888 seats. Therefore, the average population size of each constituency would have to increase. The number of Lok Sabha seats in North Indian states like Uttar Pradesh and Bihar is likely to increase significantly compared to South Indian states, as the number of seats has remained unchanged for the past 50 years. The BJP is eager for delimitation as it would strengthen its political position, particularly in North India. By linking delimitation with women's reservation, the BJP is attempting to leverage its advantage, but the opposition has not yet revealed its strategy. In this political battle, the women's reservation bill may suffer as a consequence.

### [Humans of Bombay vs People of India: What is copyright infringement and when does it apply?](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/explained-law/humans-of-bombay-vs-people-of-india-case-explained-8957397/)

The Delhi High Court has issued summons to Instagram account "People of India" (POI) in a copyright infringement suit filed by storytelling platform "Humans of Bombay" (HOB). HOB alleges that POI has imitated its storytelling style and used similar images and videos. The court found "substantial imitation" between the two platforms. Copyright infringement refers to the unauthorized use of a substantial part of a copyrighted work, and the copyright owner can take legal action for remedies such as injunctions and damages. HOB has also claimed "passing off and unfair competition" as POI has used content identical or similar to HOB's in an attempt to benefit from its goodwill and reputation. The court has issued notice to POI and listed the matter for further hearing.

### [Lok Sabha goes to a new home, two crucial matters of probity still undecided](https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/lok-sabha-code-of-conduct-business-interest-8957367/)

Hmm, I am not sure. Email support@chatbase.co for more info.Hmm, I am not sure. Email support@chatbase.co for more info.Members of Lok Sabha, the lower house of the Indian Parliament, have carried two pending matters to their new building. The first matter is the formulation of a Code of Conduct for members, which has been under consideration by the Committee on Ethics for over eight years. The second matter is the declaration of members' business interests, which currently only applies to members of Rajya Sabha, the upper house. Concerns over conflicts of interest among MPs have been raised for almost a century. The Ethics Committee in Lok Sabha has been reconstituted every year and is responsible for examining complaints of unethical conduct and formulating a Code of Conduct. In contrast, Rajya Sabha has had a 14-point Code of Conduct in place since 2005. The Rules of Procedure and Conduct of Business in Rajya Sabha also require the maintenance of a Register of Members' Interests, accessible to ordinary citizens under the Right to Information Act.
